6.10      In all the 6 WDs, the 1982 projected rates of return on net fixed
assets in operation would be between 6 and 9%; debt service coverages between
1.0 and 1.3 times; and, debt/ equity ratios would range between 78:22 and 88:12.
As the WDs' revenues increase in the subsequent years on progressively greater
utilization of the facilities built tinder the project, their financial perform-
ance is expected to show further improvements. By 1985, the projected rates of
return of the WDs are expected to range between 8 and 11% and the debt service
coverages between 1.2 and 1.5 times. During the project period, the performance
of each WD will be monitored and the performance indicators will include those
set forth in Annex 23.
F.   Water Rates
6.11      The Presidential decree stipulates that WDs shall fix rates and
charges for water as will be adequate to cover operating expenses and debt



- 27 -
service and also provide a reasonable surplus for extension and improvement
of the system. The decree empowers the WDs to prescribe and revise the
rates from time to time and vests in LWUA the power to review the rates
to ensure compliance with the above provisions.
6.12      In pursuance of the above policy, LWUA has, with the assistance of
the management consultants, developed guidelines for determination of water
rates by the WDs. These guidelines propose a rate structure based on what is
called "the revenue unit (RU) concept", which is explained in detail in Annex
24. The essential features of this rate structure are (a) a minimum monthly
charge for the first 10 m3 of metered consumption, depending on the size of
the service connection and the types of consumers - residential, overnment,
commercial, industrial and wholesale and (b) a uniform rate per mi for consump-
tion after the first 10 m3 for each type of consumer. An element of cross-
subsidy is built into the system inasmuch as the commercial and industrial
consumers are charged at twice the rate of the residential consumers.
6.13      The water rates in all the WDs, excepting Baguio, were very low and
the WDs and LWUA were convinced that they needed to be raised substantially to
achieve financial viability. The following increases have since been made by
the WDs: (a) in Tarlac WD about 80% effective November 1, 1976; (b) in Lipa
WD about 66%, in Cabanatuan 25% and in San Fernando (La Union) WD 80%, all
effective January 1, 1977; and (c) in Lucena-Pagbilao-Tayabas WD about 60%
effective April 1, 1977. In Baguio WD the water rate for commercial and
industrial consumers has been increased by about 80% effective October 1,
1976. These increases are satisfactory. The old and the new rates are shown
in Annex 25.
6.14      Significant further increases in water rates will be required in
1979 and thereafter to achieve the objective of financial viability of the
WDs. By 1982 the average rate per m3 is projected to increase to between
2.20 Pesos and 3.30 Pesos as against the 1977 estimated revenue of between
0.50 Pesos and 1.70 Pesos in the 6 WDs. Annex 22 containing assumptions
for the financial projections shows the average rates assumed for the year
1976-85 in each WD. The increases in Lipa, Lucena-Pagbilao-Tayabas and
San Fernando (La Union) WDs will be much sharper than those in Baguio,
Cabanatuan and Tarlac WDs.
6.15      LWUA's guidelines for rate determination (Annex 24) have certain
inequities. The rate structure based on the guidelines results in a higher
average charge per m3 for the first 10 m3 of monthly consumption which hits
the lowest category of small residential consumers with 1/2" connections.
On the other hand, the uniform rate per m3, after the first 10 m3 fails to
discourage excessive use of water which could be possible through progres-
sively increasing block rates. Another drawback is that the objective of
rate making is to generate funds to meet the current financial costs and
not the much higher economic costs of future development of water sources.



- 28 -
Unless the real cost of supplying "additional" water is transmitted to the
consumer, by means of a tariff based on incremental future costs, the consumer
will continue to increase his consumption as though future water sources were
as inexpensive to develop as the past ones, which is not true for the 6 WDs.
A review of the guidelines is therefore necessary. LWUA will carry out this
review, in consultation with the Bank, and by June 1978 formulate revised
guidelines for observance by the Water Districts.
6.16      The WDs will make an annual review of their water tariffs, in
consultation with LWUA, and based upon LWUA's latest guidelines to determine
what changes if any, are needed in their water rate structures and the level
of charges. In the 5 years 1978 through 1982, the review will be completed
and sent to the Bank by October 1 in each year and the agreed changes in rates
will be implemented by January 1 of the following year. After 1982 the
intervals at which the annual reviews will be sent to the Bank will be agreed
with LUWA on the basis of the financial and operating conditions of each WD.
6.17      During the project construction period, the rates will be increased
gradually and be designed to generate sufficient cash surplus (after meeting
the operating costs and maintenance and debt service 1/) to make a contribu-
tion of at least 10% to the investment on the water supply project (para
6.06). This level of contribution is reasonable in the light of the present
financial positions of the WDs and the extent of construction financing avail-
able from LWUA, including the proceeds of the proposed Bank loan. A level
substantially higher would require tariffs to be increased unrealistically.
For convenience in ascertaining the extent of each year's construction ex-
ditures to be financed from internal resources, the contribution will be
measured during 1978, 1979 and 1980 by taking 9% of the year-end amount of
the project loans, except that in San Fernando (La Union) WD the contribution
for 1980 will be about 5%. In San Fernando (La Union) WD the project expendi-
tures in 1977-79 would be about 69% of the total project cost for that WD
while in the other 5 WDs, the corresponding percentages would be between
47 and 58. San Fernando (La Union) WD's outstanding loan at the end of 1979
will be proportionately greater and therefore a lower percentage for 1980 is
justified.
6.18      Beginning with 1982 the Water Districts will be faced with major
debt service payments which will greatly increase their cash requirements.
By that time, therefore, the WDs should set and maintain water rates which
will generate sufficient revenues to cover (a) operating costs including
maintenance and depreciation; (b) debt service to the extent it exceeds the
provision for depreciation and (c) expenditures on normal expansion and
improvement of the systems. To achieve this objective the WDs will earn a
rate of return on net fixed assets of at least 6% in 1982, 7% in 1983 and
8% thereafter.
6.19      The average household in the 6 WDs has about 8 family members and
consumes about 24 m (100 1/cd) of water in a month. The monthly water charge
at the estimated 1977 rates will be about 16 to 20 Pesos (US$2.1 to 2.7) in
1/   On the existing non-project loans which are small.



- 29 -
the 4 WDs of Cabanatuan, Lipa, Lucena-Pagbilao-Tayabas and San Fernando (La
Union) and about 30 Pesos (US$4.0) in Baguio and Tarlac.  An "abili"ty to pay"
survey 1/ of 556 families conducted in April-May 1975 in the city of Lipa and
the municipality of Tanuan determined the average monthly income per house-
hold at 680 Pesos (of which 28% had an average income of 220; 55% of 660,
12% of 1,000 and 5% of 2,700 Pesos). Assuming a modest 5% per annum growth
in household incomes and a 7% rate of inflation, the average monthly income in
1977 should be around 850 Pesos. The average monthly water charge will amount
to about 2.5% of average income in 4 WDs and about 4% in Baguio and Tarlac.
The projected increased rates for 1982 deflated to 1977 prices also show that
the average family would pay about 3 to 4% of monthly income towards water
charges, given a real growth of 7% per annum in incomes. Assuming that the
lowest income category family consumes about 10 m per month (42 1/cd), the
monthly water charge of about 10 to 12.5 Pesos will be about 4 to 5% of income
(except in Tarlac WD which has a minimum charge of 18.75 Pesos). The water
charges in the 6 Project WDs are comparable with the charges in other WDs in
the Phillipines and are within the consumers capacity to pay. To reduce the
burden on the lowest income category consumers, a major portion of the further
increases in rates (para 6.14) will be absorbed by the higher income categories
and by the commercial and industrial consumers.
G.   Financial Resources of LWUA
6.20      LWUA has an authorized capital of 500 million Pesos (US$66.67 mil-
lion) to be subscribed wholly by Government at a rate of not less than 50
million Pesos (US$6.67 million) each year. It can also borrow funds from
domestic and foreign sources provided that the outstanding amounts of such
loans shall not, at any time, exceed 1,000 million Pesos (US$133.33 million)
in respect of the domestic loans and US$500 million equivalent in respect of
the foreign loans. As of July 31, 1976 LWUA's subscribed capital amounted to
98.2 million Pesos (US$13.1 million). It had contracted no local loans and
its foreign loans 2/ aggregated US$44.4 million of which about US$9.7 million
was disbursed. A schedule of LWUA's foreign loans as of July 31, 1976 is at
Annex 26. Taking into account the proposed Bank loan (US$18.8 million for
parts A and B of the project) and a further US$10.0 million USAID loan expected
in 1977, LWUA's total borrowing will amount to US$73.2 million. LWUA expects
to arrange additional loans from the Bank, ADB and other sources to finance
water supply development projects in the new Water Districts. The speed of
such development will necessarily depend on completion of the feasibility
studies for the new projects and progress on the projects now under con-
struction and those proposed under the Bank loan.
6.21      On the basis of the available information, LWUA's projected financial
statements - income statements, funds flow statements and balance sheets for
the years 1976 through 1981 are shown in Annexes 27(a), (b) and (c). These
projections are based on the projects which have been identified or for which

6.22      LWUA's projected lending of about 880 million Pesos (11S$117.4
million) during 1977-81 will be met from foreign borrowings, Government
equity and operating income.  The Bank loan will meet about 16% and other
foreign loans about 40% of LWUA's requirements; Government equity will pro-
vide about 34% and the balance of about 10% will come from operating income
and repayments by WDs.



- 31 -
6.23      The average cost of LWUA's present loans is about 4%.  Including
the proposed Bank loan and a likely further loan of US$10.0 million from USAID
in 1977, the average cost would be about 5%. If the interest-free share capi-
tal expected to be subscribed by the Government through 1981 is taken into
account and no further borrowings are assumed through that year, LWUA's cost
of capital would be about 3%. However, if LWUA borrowed another US$73 mil-
lion (equal to the current estimated borrowing - para 6.20), at about 9% p.a.,
its cost of borrowing will rise to about 7% and cost of capital (i.e. includ-
ing interest-free Government equity) to about 5%. LWUA's present lending rate
of 9% p.a. is therefore expected to provide an adequate margin for meeting
its operating costs. The financial forecasts show that LWUA would earn net
surpluses of between 4 and 8% on its equity during the years 1977 through
1981 and that its debt/equity ratio will be 1:1 or better. Thus, LWUA's
financial outlook is satisfactory. However, LWUA's authorized borrowing
limit of US$633 million equivalent (para 6.20) which is over 9 times its
authorized capital, is too high. If LWUA borrowed funds to this limit,
without a corresponding increase in its capital, its heavy debt obligation
could seriously affect its financial position. To guard against heavy un-
debtedness LWUA will maintain a debt:equity ratio of not more than 3:1.
6.24      LWUA's debt service payments to the Government on the proposed
Bank loan (which will be relent by the Government to LWUA and will be
repaid in 20 years including 4-1/2 years grace period) will be about US$2.8
million in 1982 and will gradually decrease thereafter. The six WD's annual
repayments to LWUA over the 25-1/2 years i.e., 30 years maturity including
4-1/2 years grace period, will be about US$3.4 million equivalent. Thus,
it is unlikely that LWUA will have any difficulty in servicing the Bank
loan with the shorter maturity, unless there are major defaults in the
WDs' repayments to LWUA. With a view to keeping each project WD's in-
debtedness within reasonable limits the WD will not (a) during the project
construction period, undertake any major capital expenditures other than
those included in the project and (b) after project completion, incur any
long-term debt if its net revenues for the preceding fiscal year or for
a later twelve-month period prior to such incurrence, are less than 1.5
times the maximum debt service requirement on all its long-term indebted-
ness in any succeeding fiscal year.
VII. AGREEMENTS REACHED AND RECOMMENDATIONS
7.01      Agreement has been reached with Government, LWUA and NWSS on the
following principal points which have been recorded as covenants in the
draft legal documents:
(i)  Government will relend the proceeds of the Bank loan to
LWUA and to MWSS on the same terms as the Bank loan.
LWUA will in turn, relend to WDs the proceeds of the
Bank loan for the water supply project on terms and
conditions satisfactory to the Bank, and will also make
loans to them on the same terms to meet the local currency
costs of the project and interest during construction
(paras 4.01 and 6.07).



- 32 -
(ii)  In consultation with LWUA, each WD will carry out an annual
review of its water tariffs and implement the conclusions
of such reviews by January 1 of the following year (para 6.16).
(iii)  Each WD will meet at least 10% of its project investment from
its internal cash generation (para 6.17).
(iv)  The WD's rate of return on net fixed assets will be at least
6% in 1982; 7% in 1983 and 8% thereafter (para 6.18).
7.02      A condition of loan effectiveness is the execution of the two
subsidiary loan agreements on terms and conditions acceptable to the Bank,
(a) between the Government and LWUA and (b) between the Government and MWSS,
for relending the proceeds of the loan to LWUA and MWSS (para 4.01).
7.03      The project is suitable for a Bank loan of US$23.0 million for
a term of 20 years including a grace period of 4-1/2 years.
April 14, 1977

PHILIPPINES
PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Description of the Project
A. INTRODUCTION
Master Plans
1.        The Master Plans and feasibility studies for the six cities pre-
pared by consultants cover a period up to year 2000 with implementation
divided into three phases.
2.        The proposed 4 year investment plan recommended by the consultants
for Phase I, with the twin objectives of rehabilitation of the existing sys-
tems and extensions to meet immediate needs, is reasonable. However, because
of the scarce water demand data, the limited knowledge of the groundwater
hydrology, the need for the new Water Districts and LWUA to substantially
improve operational and financial performance, concern that the increases in
tariffs needed to support an investment of the size proposed would be diffi-
cult to impose and collect, the consultants' investment proposals for Baguio
and San Fernando (La Union) WDs were reduced during the appraisal by about
28% and a critical review will be made during detailed design for somewhat
smaller reductions in the order of 5% for Cabanatuan, Lipa and Tarlac WDs.
The shortfall is postponed for inclusion in a Phase II project, now timed
to commence in about 1983 for Baguio and San Fernando (La Union) and 1986
for the other WDs.
B. PROJECT DESCRIPTION
3.        The physical components of works briefly described below for con-
struction in the six water districts are shown in IBRD Maps 12543-48. Except
for the rehabilitation works, which are so described all other items represent
new works covering the construction of wells equipped with pumps, storage
tanks, transmission mains, pumping stations and distribution pipelines in
internal networks, service connections, buildings, and the procurement of
vehicles, meters and hydrants. The cost of each item of work and total proj-
ect costs for each water district are given in Annex 4, pages 2-7 and the
total cost of water supply construction is summarized in Annex 4, page 1.

4.        An early action program, mostly concerned with rehabilitation of
existing wells to be retained in the systems, metering and reduction of leak-
age aimed at improving service will be carried out in the early stages of the
project. The population and water demand projections, existing water supply
systems and the water sources on which the project is based are described in
detail in Annexes 7 and 8. The length of pipelines, storage capacity and num-
ber of wells are close to what will be needed but actual sizes, capacities,
lengths and numbers will be determined after review, field investigations and
detailed design.

C. BASIS OF COST ESTIMATES
Unit Costs
5.        Because there was no recent experience in late 1975, the cost esti-
mates in the preliminary engineering studies are based upon unit costs devel-
oped by the consultants by applying consumer price indices and retail price
indices, furnished by the Central Bank of the Philippines, to local price
components, and the Engineering News Record cost index for imported equipment.
The unit costs, however, compare favorably with bids received in mid 1976 on
some of the works to be started in the ADB project. A comparison of some
rounded-off costs for major project components at June 1976 prices for Baclod
City Water District are given below:
Consultants'
Estimate          Bid #1         Bid #2          Bid #3
Pipelines/m
100-600 mm                  73-970          36-748         37-836          39-730

6.        With regard to well construction, a comparison was made of the cost
curves developed for making the estimates with recent bids received for 100 m
deep wells. In San Pablo the bid for a 150 mm diameter well was lower than
the estimate by P 12,000 per meter. On the other hand, in Batangas, the bid
for a 250 mm diameter well was higher than the estimate by P 5000 per meter.
The estimates are reasonable.
Contingencies
7.        Because of the difficult terrain in Baguio physical contingencies
are assumed to be 20% of construction costs, including engineering. For all
of the other WDs physical contingencies are assumed to be 15%, normally used
in such projecLs, as no special construction problems are anticipated.
8.        Price contingencies assume a 10% increase over the first two years
and 8% thereafter on all costs for the six cities, both on equipment and
civil works which are roughly equal. This is based on the assumption that
both local and foreign cost inflation will be about the same and would follow
the present downward trend over the next four years.



ANNEX 1
Page 7
D. REVIEW, FIELD INVESTIGATIONS AND DETAILED DESIGN
Technical Review
9.        Consultants to be appointed and financed under the project will be
responsible for making the review of the CDM and JMM preliminary engineering
studies, carrying out field investigations, preparing revised cost estimates,
making detailed designs, supervising construction and training Filipino staff.
It is estimated that about 144 man-months of expatriate consulting services
will be needed for these tasks. Some items needing particular attention
during the review are given in the following paragraphs.
10.       The review will include updating of all water production and con-
sumption statistics and revising the demand projections. The results of
the leakage surveys as part of the field investigations (see para 15 below)
should be reflected in the revised demand projections. For Baguio WD, in
addition, an assessment will be made of operating and maintenance costs fol-
lowing the recent acquisition from Baguio Municipality of a small hydroelec-
tric power station. In Cabanatuan the review should include the proposed
water supply in the resettlement area.
11.       In all of the cities, the technical and economic feasibility of
postponing or doing away with the provision of storage should be studied and
the project costs revised accordingly before detailed design. If storage
is found to be necessary, design alternatives should be considered between
elevated storage and standpipes. For all project components, design alterna-
tives should be considered on the basis of the more accurate consumption
statistics now available.
Field Investigations
12.       Field investigations are needed in Baguio to confirm the preliminary
engineering findings that the aquifer in La Trinidad is the most productive.
On the basis of test well results and review of the pollution hazard, the pre-
liminary designs made in 1973 should be reviewed and alternative system config-
urations studied and a recommendation made for the least-cost solution. In
Lipa a test well should be drilled to confirm the theoretical assessment of
groundwater availability to support 15.6 1/sec wells including the reasons
for the recent failure of the public market well to meet the consultants'
forecasts. The consultants should recommend drilling of other test wells
on the basis of their technical reviews of the preliminary groundwater hydrol-
ogy studies.
13.       In San Fernando (La Union) it is proposed that the project be fur-
ther staged, and the Lon-oy river source retained. The technical feasibility
of this proposal will require studies on low-cost treatment of the Lon-oy
water, possibly by utilizing slow sand filters and rehabilitating the trans-
mission pipeline (as proposed for the Santo Thomas scheme in Baguio). The
proposed further staging will require a detailed review of the water demand



ANNEX 1
Page 8
centers between Bauang and San Fernando and, based upon more accurate metered
consumption statistics, an evaluation made of the economic feasibility of
postponing storage and furthei phasing transmission pipelines in achieving
the lest-cost solution.
14.       In all of the cities the areas of high demand and future growth
would be carefully identified to enable the placement of the distribution
networks provided in the project in areas where demand for new service con-
nections is greatest to achieve the projected number of service connections-,
15.       The field investigation will include leak detection surveys for
two months and during that time the consultants would train Filipino staff,
set up regular procedures for leakage surveys and also review the conclu-
sions on leakage in the preliminary engineering studies.
Detailed Design
16.       Before proceeding to detailed design the design criteria in the
preliminary engineering studies would be reviewed on the basis of the field
investigations and the criteria for detailed design agreed with LWUA. At
the same time the unit costs for all of the major project components would
be agreed on the basis of actuals in the ongoing projects and a revised cost
estimate made for the works. When making the revised cost estimates, steps
would be taken to prequalify contractors for the project and also a study
made of the feasibility of project construction on either the basis of sep-
arating procurement of materials and equipment from construction or for com-
bining these in supply and lay contracts. Besides economic feasibility cri-
teria, past experience and special local circumstances would be considered
in the study as discussed in paragraph 22 below.
17.       In reviewing the network analysis made in the preliminary engineer-
ing studies the agreed design criteria based on the field investigations
would be utilized to arrive at the best technical and economic configuration
for the water supply systems and the preliminary designs made accordingly.
Preliminary design of the intake structures for the May-it Spring source, the
river intakes and treatment plant (if the Lon-oy source is found to be
feasible) for San Fernando, wells, sump and booster pump station designs for
the other sources would be made and agreed as would all proposals for the
sizing of storage facilities wherever these are needed.
18.       The topographic surveys for the pipeline design and soil investiga-
tions for the major civil construction works, such as reservoirs, would be
done in sufficient detail so that the contractors will have adequate informa-
tion for pricing their bids.



ANNEX 1
Page 9
C. CONSTRUCTION
Supervision of Construction
19.       The consultants would be responsible for preparing the draft pre-
qualification and post-qualification forms, tender advertisement for publica-
tion abroad, invitation to tender, description of the tendering and evaluation
procedures, conditions of contract, specification, bills of quantities, tender
forms and all related documents required for the purposes of international
competitive bidding. On receipt of the tenders the consultants will make a
detailed evaluation and submit a report making recommendations to LWUA on
the acceptance of bids.
20.       While being fully responsible for the construction of the facili-
ties as specified, particular attention would be given to the well-drilling
program, with the objective of drilling, constructing and developing wells
for the specified yields and long-term service. Special attention will be
given also to the construction of pipelines and service connections to avoid
future leakage developing in the system. The water district staff will be
trained in the setting of meters and the making of water-tight service con-
nections. Assistance will also be given in the construction of immediate
improvement works under the early action program.
Project Management
21.       LWUA will be responsible for carrying out the project and has
appointed a Project Manager for providing the supervision and administra-
tion of the project. (See paras 3.15 to 3.17 in the Appraisal Report.)
Construction Alternatives
22.       There is little experience at the present time on the most econom-
ical method of packaging construction contracts for water supply projects.
The method adopted in the USAID projects is to let out one complete contract
covering all the construction to be done in each major construction category
in each city, the contractor being responsible for both the procurement and
the installation of all major components. It is not clear if more economical
construction would be achieved by the procurement of all the pipes and fit-
tings in the project to be supplied to the various cities for subsequent
installation under separately placed civil works contracts as proposed in the
ADB projects. LWUA feels that there may be practical difficulties in super-
vising procurement, especially in ensuring compatibility of the pipes with
the fittings. There is also the possibility that the civil works contractors
may attribute poor workmanship to manufacturing defects in the pipes and fit-
tings.
23.       Similar alternatives are possible with the construction of wells,
particularly in regard to the procurement of well screens. The consultants
will study these various alternatives and make a recommendation to LWUA on



ANNEX I
Page 10
the metthod of packagling contracts likely to be most economical. For the pur-
poses of construction planning, it hias been assumed that a complete contract
will be let for each city. This assumption is made in the construction
schedule showi in Part A of the implementation schedule at Annex 6.
Disbursement Schedule
24.       The quarterly disbursement schedule shown in Annex 5 is based upon
the construction schedule and on the assumption that contractors will apply
for payments according to works completed in each month. Disbursements on
pipeline construction are expected to commence in early 1978, peak in 1979
and decline slo-wly to about mid-1981.  Well construction will start somewhat
later in 1978, because of the time needed to complete field investigations,
btut will be completed by mid-1980. Operational buildings are also expected
to follow the same schedule as well construction. Meters and service connec-
tions will be procured and installed from mid-1979 to end-1981.
25.       The consultanits' fees for detailed design and the various studies
in the project are assumed to be evenly distributed over the respective study
periods.

PHILIPPINES
PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Feasibility Studies for Water Supplies to Ten Cities
A. INTRODUCTION
1.        LWUA has an ambitious program for carrying out feasibility studies
and construction of water supply schemes with assistance from USAID, ADB and
the Danish International Development Agency (DANIDA). USAID financed feasi-
bility studies have been followed by the financing of construction by ADB for
five cities, USAID for five ctties and proposed IBRD financing for six cities.
DANIDA is financing waterworks development in two more cities. A recently
completed pre-feasibility study of one-hundred cities, also financed by USAID,
identified some forty to sixty cities for more detailed feasibility studies.
USAID is financinag feasibility studies for ten of these cities and it is now
proposed that IBRD finance the feasibility studies for ten more of these
cities.
2.        In making the feasibility studies for these ten additional cities
LWUA is anxious to continue the staff training started with the USAID studies.
It also wishes to modify the earlier approach by (1) shortening the study
period (2) preparing and implementing an immediate impact program of works,
and (3) including detailed design and preparation of bid documents in the
studies. This new approach is reasonable because of the experience on such
common matters such as development of current costs, design criteria and demand
projections. The terms of reference and the conditions of employment of the
consultants to be selected for these studies should be acceptable to the Bank.
B. SELECTION OF CITIES FOR STUDY
3.        Listed below are sixteen of the forty cities identified in the USAID
studies:

Manila Sewerage Design Study
A. INTRODUCTION
1.        A Study entitled "Master Plan for a Sewerage System for the Manila
Metropolitan Area" was carried out by MWSS with assistance from UNDP. The
initial study started in December 1967 and was concluded in December 1970.
The study was prepared by a US consulting firm, Black and Veatch International
(BVI), working under an agreement with WHO as executing agency.
2.        The study made recommendations on Administration and Operations
and also a first stage construction program to serve central Manila estimated
at US$72 million equivalent. 1/ Allowing for the additional growth that has
taken place in the intervening years, and based upon prices developed in the
sewerage component of the ongoing sewerage construction in the Tondo slum
upgrading in the Manila Urban Development Project, MWSS estimate that the
cost of carrying out a first stage expansion program will be nearer US$170
million equivalent (P 1,270 million) - an increase of 142%.  The preliminary
estimate is as follows:

to benefit in the near future from the sewerage project. Because of the com-
plex nature of the studies a technical advisor to the Filipino Project Manager,
represnting a total of 24 man-months, is proposed to insure the correct execu-
tion and satisfactory completion of the studies. There will be a total of
453 man-months of expatriate consulting services for the studies.
6.        The study will commence with a review and the identification of
alternatives for more detailed study. At this point MWSS will obtain advice
from an expert review panel, the costs of which are included in the studies.
The panel will review the documents produced by the consultants and then meet
in Manila to provide guidance on the choice of the best alternative for



ANNEX 3
Page 3
the preparation of detailed designs. The average man-month cost of the
expatriate consultants' services is estimated to be about US$6,200 excluding
travel and local costs. This is reasonable in view of the complex nature of
the tasks to be performed.
7.        The estimated costs of the consulting services are as follows:

8.        The consulting services for sewerage design only is US$5.8 million
and is 3.4% of the estimated construction cost of the future project. This
is reasonable.
C. TERMS OF REFERENCE
9.        The following is an extract from the draft terms of reference for
the proposed study covering the review, identification of alternatives and
the development of a revised sewerage master plan.
Part A:   Review Services and Preliminary Studies
Al. Review Services
This part of the consultant's services shall cover the review
and evaluation of the following:
Concepts
(a) The proposed basic concepts in earlier feasibility
reports, in particular the proposed method of sewage
collection and disposal; the identification of priori-
ties and the selection of the scope of the first stage
program.



ANNEX 3
Page 4
Sanitation
(b) The available data on existing sanitary conditions.
Sewerage
(c) The 1969/70 Sewerage Master Plan and other existing
relevant reports; subsequent oceanographic survey
reports and data; maps, records and record drawings;
and any other relevant documents concerning the existing
situation in the study areas as defined in the Master
Plan.
(d) On-going and planned programs and projects within the
Metrolpolitan Manila Area (MMA) to the extent to which
these may affect sewerage planning and construction.
These programs and projects include: water supply
projects; land reclamation projects; urban renewal;
town planning; housing and industrial development
programs; drainage; flood and pollution control proj-
ects (including in particular the Manganhan floodway,
the Napindan hydraulic control structure, the Laguna de
Bay Interceptors and the Paranaque Spillway) and
highway projects including tunnels, bridges, skyways
and interchanges.
(e) Design criteria proposed in the 1969/70 Master Plan,
in the light of the consultants' work under (c) and
(d) above, particularly those relating to: existing
population; population growth, density and distribu-
tion; and waste water quantities and characteristics.
(f) Existing sewer system, plants and other facilities
within the study area.
On completion of the above and the preliminary sanitation field
survey described in section D12 1/ the Consultant shall submit a Review
and Evaluation Report which shall include assessments of the following:
Sanitation
(i) Existing sanitary conditions in the study area and identi-
fication of high priority areas - especially low income
high density slum areas that require immediate attention.
(ii) The relationship between existing water supply levels and
proposed waste disposal methods.
1/   Section D12 is not included in this extract.



ANNEX 3
Page 5
(iii) Applicability of basic planning concepts and design criteria
on environmental sanitation in the earlier reports.
(iv) Adequacy of existing data and information and specific
need for any supplemental or supportive information such
as needed for any further work (site investigations, toilet
design, establishment of component manufacturers) in
identifying alternatives.
Sewerage
(v) The adequacy of existing data and information, and the need
for any supplemental information.
(vi) The viability and practicability of basic planning concepts
and design criteria, insofar as sewage collection and dis-
posal are concerned;
(vii) The validity of program priorities and strategies previously
proposed;
A2. Preliminary Alternative Studies
As a result of his review under Al, the consultant shall develop
studies directed towards conceptualization of an overall sanitation and
sewerage scheme for the study area with various alternatives to the basic
design concepts embodied in earlier documents. These preliminary alterna-
tive studies shall include:
Sanitation
(a) Alternative sanitation schemes appropriate for each region
and sub-area (water-borne sewerage systems or other non-
conventional methods) as well as immediate sanitation schemes
including water, footpaths and minor drainage that will have
significant impact in improving sanitation.
(b) Preparation of sketch designs of feasible alternatives, to
include low cost waste disposal systems (pit privies, aqua
privies, etc.) that are socially acceptable and compatible
with public health aspects as well as conventional waterborne
reticulation systems, with preliminary cost estimates for
capital and operational and maintenance aspects involved in
each alternative with emphasis where applicable, on local
construction materials (bamboo, clay pipes, etc.) self-help,
and community sharing of costs.
(c) Evaluation of the proposed alternatives in terms of their
technical viability, social acceptability, associated health
benefits, and general cost effectiveness (i.e., maximization
of health benefits per units of expenditure);



ANNEX 3
Page 6
(d) Description of nature (short-term, interim, or long-term)
of the recommended alternative for each region and sub-area
and its relationship to the long-term goals;
Sewerage
(a) Delineation of sewer drainage basins and their subdivisions;
(b) the disposal of raw or partially treated sewage by means
of a submarine outfall or outfalls, as opposed to various
degrees of treatment at one or more sites, combined with
various forms of effluent and sludge disposal. This shall
include the identification of possible alternative disposal
sites, consistent with future plans for land and water
development, and recommendation on effluent quality standards
appropriate to the uses of the receiving waters. It shall
also include recommendations on the priorities and phasing
of any proposed treatment facilities in relation to the
expansion of sewered areas and the anticipated build up of
waste water flow, potential ultimate reuse, such as the
recapture of nutrients by aquaculture and recycling of
sludge having regard to energy requirements and the likely
financial constraints on the overall program.
(c) Examination of alternative alignments, elevations and
design concepts for the trunk sewers and interceptors,
together with ancillary structures such as pumping
stations and siphons. The examination shall include a
study of alternative methods of interceptor construction
(tunneling versus cut-and-cover); and evaluation of the
applicability of combined storm and sanitary sewers
(either generally or in certain areas): possible alter-
natives to the Pasig interceptor and vortex structures
recommended in the 1969 report; and an examination of the
benefits of minimizing pumping of sewage, or of concen-
trating pumping at one or more central points.
(d) Each alternative shall be costed, giving, construction and
recurrent costs (in constant prices), divided between local
and foreign costs both direct and indirect and discounted at
8, 10 and 12%. The consultants shall make preliminary recom-
mendations on the least cost solutions for each alternative.
On completion of Parts Al and A2 the consultant will submit a pre-
liminary draft report in two sections covering the Sanitation Program and the
Sewerage studies as follows:



ANNEX 3
Page 7
Sanitation
(i) The section of the report dealing with sanitation shall
contain recommendations for a specific program that can
be implemented over a three year period. This report
shall identify all the components recommended for each
area including, as appropriate, human waster disposal,
water supply minor footpath and drainage works, etc.
Detailed recommendations for institutional arrangements
for implementing the program including completion of
designs, construction, implementation and monitoring
shall be made. Insofar as this program is designed to
provide immediate sanitation benefits to the low income
groups, the infrastructural provisions under the pro-
gram should be kept simple yet effective. Following
review by the government the consultant will submit a
draft final report including any agreed changes. The
final report will be produced upon approval by govern-
ment of the draft final report.
Sewerage
(ii) MWSS will convene an Expert Review Panel to consider the
work of the consultant to date and to give guidance on the
future direction of the work and selected alternatives to
be studied in greater detail.
A3. Development of Revised Sewerage Master Plan
Based on the reviews and preliminary studies carried out under Al
and A2 above, and the specialized studies carried out under Section D (par-
ticularly those relating to organization and staffing; finance; and tariffs),
and on the findings and recommendations of the Expert Review Panel, the con-
sultant shall prepare an outline of a revised long-term development plan for
sewage collection and disposal for MMA, including an outline of the works to
be constructed in each phase; the target population to be served: the esti-
mated capital and operating expenses and the financial implications of these
(in particular on the tariffs needed to support the project); and the insti-
tutional development of MWSS necessary at each stage to effectively execute
and operate the program.
A4. Identification of First-Phase Sewerage Program
The consultant shall identify, within the long-term development
program, construction and rehabilitation works that should be considered
for a priority first-phase program. At least three alternative packages
shall be presented, of differing sizes, and in sufficient detail to provide
the information necessary to decide which should be adopted and developed
in greater detail. Each package should be covered by a technical and
financial feasibility study in sufficient detail to permit appraisal by
an international financing agency and for this purpose, the data required
by the World Bank "Questionnaire for Sewerage Projects", should be regarded
as a minimum.



ANNEX 3
Page 8
On completion of Parts A3 and A4 and submission of a draft report,
MWSS will decide upon the first-phase program to be adopted, and will give
the necessary instructions to the consultant to proceed with Part B of the
services.
Part B.   Preparation of Final Designs and Tender Documents for
First-Phase Sewerage Program
Bl.       Following completion of Part A, and on receipt of the instructions
as to the package of works to be included in first phase program, the consul-
tant shall proceed with the preparation of the appropriate final designs,
drawings, specifications, bills of quantities, cost estimates and other tender
documents for the procurement of goods and construction services.
B2.       The services to be performed under this Part B shall include, but
not be limited to, the following:
(i) The preparation of design criteria;
(ii) Assistance in preparation of land acquisition plans;
(iii)  Assistance and recommendation in the prequalification of
contractors for the civil engineering works;
(iv)  The preparation of detailed designs, specifications and
tender documents (including invitation to bid, bidding
instructions, form and conditions of contract, bill of
quantities, detailed engineer's estimates and preliminary
PERT-CPM construction and procurement schedules, drawings
and other related documents) for the civil engineering
works and the procurement of goods through international
competitive bidding in accordance with current World Bank
"Guidelines for Procurement";
(v)  Advising MWSS on tenders, prices and estimates for the
carrying out of the contracts for the implementation
of the Project provided that no tender shall be called
or accepted or order placed by the Consultant, except on
behalf of and with approval of MWSS;
(vi) Assistance in the evaluation of tenders and the making of
recommendations for the award of contracts;
(vii)  The preparation of working drawings for the construction
of the civil engineerng works; and
(viii)  The performance of other miscellaneous works not otherwise
specifically mentioned herein but are necessary and incidental
to the proper design of the Project including where appro-
priate, the preparation of terms of reference, contract docu-
ments, etc. for employing third parties and assistance to



ANNEX 3
Page 9
MWSS in inviting proposals, evaluating bids, awarding con-
tracts and supervising the services to be provided by the
third part(s) who may be called upon to undertake data
collection, surveying, mapping, soils investigations, testing,
etc.
10.       The terms of reference also include special studies covering (i) the
evaluation of oceanographic programs, (ii) capacity of the local sewer pipe
manufacturing industry, (iii) the preparation of a planned preventive main-
tenance program, (iv) reduction of infiltration, (v) control of industrial
waste discharges into sewers, (vi) tariff studies, (vii) financing of house
sewer connections, and (viii) a waste water laboratory and a sanitation sur-
vey including institutions for the sanitation programs.
11.       The total study period is expected to be for 24-months commencing
in the third quarter of calendar year 1977. The consultants for the study
will be selected from a shortlist prepared by MWSS and should be
acceptable to the Bank.

PHILIPPINES
PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Existing Water Supply Systems and Sources
A. INTRODUCTION
Location
1.        All of the six project cities are situated on the Island of Luzon
and have reasonably good communication and access to Metropolitan Manila the
nation's capital. Baguio and La Union have airports and the others are up to
three hours by road. The locations of the cities are shown in IBRD Map
12542.
Historical Note
2.        Lipa water supply system is the most recent, the first development
made in 1932 and Baguio the oldest, going back to 1909. Four of the cities
having access to upland catchments, developed springs and provided gravity
supplies in the early years. In the other two, because of the high water
table shallow well supplies preceded pumped schemes. Cast iron pipes con-
veyed the water to small storage reservoirs for distribution through unmetered
service connections. Except for Lucena, there is no information on any wide
spread past use of public faucets, as these now exist only in a few places in
the other five cities. The major components of the existing systems are shown
in IBRD Maps 12543 to 12548.
3.        Recent studies made by the consultants of some 100 urban water sup-
plies show that the existing waterworks systems in most of the provincial
cities and towns of the Philippines are inadequate and unreliable. This is
also true of the six cities in the project. The waterworks facilities are
generally in rundown condition and in constant disrepair because expansion of
transmission, treatment and storage facilities made since the end of World
War II had been poorly planned and badly designed. Most water distribution
networks are old and inadequately sized with leaking pipes and service con-
nections, low pressures and marginal fire fighting capabilities. Some of the
systems suffered war damage, with structural weakening of the networks and
reservoirs. Because water must be rationed, pressures fluctuate over a wide
range and often become negative, with a constant risk of contaminated water
entering the system. The facilities are improperly operated and since the
water is seldom chlorinated it is of doubtful quality.



ANNEX 7
Page 2
B. BAGUIO WATER DISTRICT
Existing Water Supply Facilities
4.        At the time of formation of the Baguio Water District (BWD) in
July 1975, the city obtained its water supply from 18 low-capacity deep
wells, a major surface diversion from the Santo Thomas River (Hydro I com-
plex) and numerous other relatively minor springs and surface diversions.
Flow from the majority of the surface and spring sources is pumped to storage
or pumped directly into the distribution grid. In 1976, BWD initiated an
interim improvement program aimed at moderately increasing production cap-
ability, reducing power costs, upgrading disinfection methods and reducing
wastage of water through metering. Specific improvements include one new well
(Test Well No. 2) with a pumping capacity of 50.4 1/sec, replacement of most
of the existing pumping units, installation of new   chlorination units and
metering of all customer services.
5.        The water supply system includes a 3.72 ha catchment basin near the
top of Mount Santo Thomas, but leakage from the Santo Thomas impounding rese-
rvoir is so large (estimated to vary from 63-126 l/sec) that soon after the
rainy season the reservoir becomes empty. The transmission and distribution
systems include two booster stations and approximately 75 km of pipe ranging
in diameters from 25 mm (galvanized iron) to 400 mm (steel). As more than
50 percent of the mains were installed prior to World War II leakage is high
and most of these pipes require replacement.  There a5e seven distribution
storage reservoirs, with a total capacity of 18,400 m . Because of their
awkward elevations, some of the existing reservoirs are not able to function
properly and will be retired in the future. The customer service piping is
generally galvanized iron and in relatively poor condition. All the meters
are new and of acceptable quality.
6.        In summary, the principal deficiencies of the existing system are
(1) high water losses due to leaks in old distribution pipes and service
lines (2) inadequate hydraulic capacity in the primary network (3) reservoirs
located at differing elevations without established pressure zones which hin-
der efficient operation of the system and (4) inadequate source capacity.
The extent of leakage is unknown but, because of the greater pressures in the
hilly terrain, is expected to be more than in the other WDs in the project and
is probably most of the unaccounted for water, now estimated at 60%.
Pressure Zones
7.        Three pressure zones are proposed below elevation 1,355 m for
development in the future, as these areas become populated. Four presssure
zones (IV, V, VI and VII) are proposed for immediate establishment in the
populated areas above elevation 1,355 m, each zone with an appropriate



ANNEX 7
Page 3
maximum working pressure of 7 kgf/cm  1/.  Two 3,785 m reservoirs are pro-
posed for construction in the project to serve zones IV and V at the Hospital
and Camp Allen where sites are now available. Pressure zones VI and VII in
the uppermost elevations will in this phase be served by the existing reser-
voirs and direct pumping into the system. Additional storage at Upper Pacdal,
Lower Pacdal and Quezon Hill will be provided in the next phase to serve
these upper zones when suitable sites become available. During detailed
design a review will be made of the proposed pressure zones and alterna-
tives for serving these zones studied for achieving greatest economy in
capital and operating costs. The capacities and control elevations of the
existing reservoirs and proposed elevations and capacities of future reser-
voirs for serving these upper elevations are as follows:

9.        Of these sources 8 are either uneconomical or contaminated and
will be retired and the leaking Santo Thomas reservoir repaired. The total
dry season yield of the sources that will be retained is 315.2 l/sec,
excluding Test Well No. 2 which is expected to have a standby function.
Water Resources Studies
10.       Baguio is situated on a highland plateau with elevations ranging
from 1300 to 1600 meters above sea level in the mountains of Benguet Pro-
vince. During 1975/76 James M. Montgomery (JMM), Consulting Engineers, Inc.
(US) made an evaluation of Baguio water resources as a supplement to a water
supply feasibility study made in 1973 by consulting engineers, Adrain
Wilson and JMM. The evaluation includes a field inventory of potential
sources of supply, geological investigations leading to the preparation of
a geological map, and a test well program.   A preliminary study of the city's
waste water disposal practices was also made to determine the dangers of pol-
lution to the water resources.
Waste Water Disposal
11.       Baguio city's waste water is mostly discharged into the Balili
river to the north thereby polluting the limestone aquifers (identified
as having the greatest potential for development) and also causing health
hazards to downstream users. The city has therefore recently started con-
struction of 130 1/sec sewage treatment plant estimated to cost P 50 million.
Planning of future water development can be therefore based on the assumption
that pollution of the northern aquifer would be substantially reduced.
During a review of the water resources evaluation to be made by the design
consultants for the project the economic feasbility of simultaneously developing
both the northern and southern limestone aquifers would be studied before pro-
ceeding to detailed design. (See para 17).
Hydrology
12.       The weather in the Baguio region varies between two prolonged wet
and dry seasons. The period from May through October experiences high rainfall
whereas the climatic conditions from November through April become hot and
dry. Wet season rainfall is influenced by the strong southwest monsoons
and cyclonic storms. Average annual rainfall in Baguio City during the
last 25 years has equaled 3,700 mm. Wide variations in annual rainfall have
long been observed and it is not uncommon for precipitation to exceed or
fall short of the annual figure by 200 percent.
13.       The highland plateau on which Baguio is situated has four major
drainage systems, These are the north flowing Baguio-La Trinidad Asin-Tuba
drainage systems and the south trending Bued River and Agno River drainage
systems. Th! total tributary drainage area covered by the four systems is
about 240 km . Within this area, elevations range from 600 to 2,250 meters



ANNEX 7
Page 6
above sea level. Topographic conditions vary from mild to moderate on the
plateaus to extremely rugged on the slope and ridges below.
14.       The ground water regime of the Baguio plateau consists of a complex
of several aquifers which have a combined area of about 25 km2. Five prin-
cipal zones of water bearing formations have been identified by the consul-
tants. On the plateau and within the City, two main aquifers named as the
"Shallow Aquifer" and "Baguio Aquifer" have been identified. The Shallow
Aquifer is composed of tight, fine grained alluvial materials with limited
storage and low permeability characteristics. The extent of this aquifer is
limited to the Burnham Park area within the city and La Trinidad Valley on the
outskirts. Beneath the Shallow Aquifer lies the more extensive Baguio Aquifer
occupying much of the plateau area at depths varying from 15 to 50 meters
below the ground surface. The Baguio Aquifer is composed of moderately to
strongly hardened sandstone and conglomerate beds and much of the water bear-
ing capability of this aquifer results from fracture or bedding plane permea-
bility. All but three of the existing production wells have been drilled in
the Shallow and Baguio Aquifers. Production rates of these wells vary from
less than 6 to more than 25 1/sec. This large variation has resulted pri-
marily from site location, construction methods as well as size and depth of
the wells.
15.       Three other important aquifers which are described below are located
on the flanks of the plateau to the south and west of the City have been identi-
fied. The "Camp 7 Aquifer" is found in the large canyon between Camp 7 and 8.
This aquifer is known to be at least 130 meters thick covering an area of at
least 2 sq km and consists of fine to coarse sand and sandstone interfingered
with clay and tight fine-grained material. Beneath the Camp 7 Aquifer lies a
fine grained unconsolidated sand that has been named the "Deep Aquifer". The
total thickness and extent of this aquifer has not been fully determined due
to limited subsurface exploration. In the area of exploration the aquifer
is confined and wells penetrating this zone can yield free-flowing water. A
fifth major aquifer is formed by the Mirador Limestone found in areas north-
west (in La Trinidad valley) and to the southwest of the city. The limestone
is known to have a thickness in excess of 300 meters in certain locations but
water bearing characteristics vary widely and reflect the degree of solution
weathering which the rock has undergone. The geologic investigations resulted
in a decision that test wells should be drilled in the limestone formations
found in the northern and southern areas of the city as having the greatest
potential for development. However, because the test well sites in the
northern areas in La Trinidad valley lay outside BWD limits, and the pollu-
tion of the limestone aquifer by waste water, (para 11 above) the test wells
were all drilled in the south. Of the four that were drilled two wells which
successfully penetrated the Camp 7 Aquifer and Mirador limestone proved suc-
cessful. One well (Test Well No 2) situated in Bakakeng Creek and penetra-
ting the Camp 7 Aquifer is now functioning as a production well with a pumping
capacity of 50.4 I/sec. The consultants have recommended a well field of
nine wells in this area (to meet 1985 demand) with a total capacity of 221
1/sec.



A.NNLX 7
Page 7
16.       As the aquifers are not homogenous and isotropic aquifer coef-
ficients based on the standard Theis equations have little signifiicance and
account for the big variations in the transmissivity coefficients calculated
by the consultants. The Baguio Aquifer transmissivity ranges from 7.5 to
311 cubic meters per day per meter (600 to 25,000 US gallons per day per foot)
while the Mirador Limestone varies from 12.5 and 2,140 meters per day per
meter (1,000 and 220,000 US gallons per day per foot). The direction of
groundwater movement is in the same general direction as the surface drainage
pattern, except where faulting and structural weaknesses have imposed local
disruptions in both direction and flow as in the Mirador Limestone where
high permeabilities have developed on such lines of weaknesses.
Water Balance Studies
17.       Of the sixteen drainage systems identified by the consultants in
the plateau seven were judged to have the highest potential for development
on the basis of a comparison of criteria such as type of supply, quantity
available, water quality, distance from Baguio, elevation differences, acces-
sibility, degree of difficulty in development and water rights. An attempt
was then made to study the water balance and a summary prepared for these
seven systems by computing values of drainge area characteristics, inflow to
basin, outflow from basin, potential perenial ground water yield and existing
additional ground water, to arrive at the potential additional water available.
This last amount wgs 5omputed to be highest in the north of the city in La
Trinidad 15.6 x 10 m /year. The next highest potential availability of water
wag i4  the south of the city (east o5 Test Well No. 2) below Camp 8 at 3.9 x
10 m /year, followed by 2.9 x 10 m /year at Test Well No. 2.
Design Alternatives for Water Source Development
18.       There are two design alternatives:  (1) develop the southern aquifer
to meet 1985 demand even in areas of northern Baguio (2) develop the southern
aquifer to meet demand in the southern areas and the northern aquifer in La
Trinidad valley to meet demand in the northern areas of the city. The consul-
tants have, without a cost comparison study, recommended the first alternative
because the potential well sites to the north are outside BWD limits and the
existing pollution of the aquifer by waste water in the Balili River. Before
commencing detailed design therefore, a cost comparison study will be made
between these alternatives on the basis of the latest available information.
This study will take into account the new sewage treatment facility, the recent
acquisition by BWD of three small hydropower stations, the reduction of leakage
arising from the replacement of the old network and updated demand projections.
The source development in the south of the city proposed by the consultants has
therefore been divided into two stages, the first of which is in the Phase I
project. The second stage source development would be undertaken only if the
cost comparison study justifies this course of action.



ANNEX 7
Page 8
19.       T-he source development included in the Phase I project consists
of a miiufnv of four production wells, (in a well field situated to the east
of Test WelL No. 2) with a combined capacity of 126.2 1/sec. The reduction
in source capacilty will be offset by increased effectiveness in the control of
leakage .iti significant reductions in energy costs.  The well field will
be located a-ound a ground level covered sump into which will discharge the
low head deei, well turbine pumps.  This 1140 m3 capacity sump will provide
for chloriniation and be the intake for the high head booster pumps that will
deliver the uwater to the system.
Water Demand
20.       With the completion of the Phase I project total water production
capacity will increase from 315.2 1/sec to about 441.4 1/sec. Including Test
Well No. 2 which has a standby role and a dry season yield of 50.4 1/sec the
total installed capacity will be 491.8 1/sec. Maximum day water demand is
however expected to increase to 400 I/sec in 1985. Peak hour demand will be
484 1/sec by 1983 increasing to 533 1/sec in 1985. The two 3785 m3 storage
tanks in the project, and Kilometer 8 storage tank will adequately provide for
meeting peak hour demand in pressure zones IV and V. In pressure zones VI and
VII however, on Quezon Hill and Upper Pacdal there may be some peak hour
shortages after 1982 according to the preliminary design. The proposed design
review by the consultants is expected to make recommendations on the need
for increasing storage in the Phase II Project.

C. CABANATUAN WATER DISTRICT
Existing Water Supply Facilities
22.       Nine out of ten existing deep wells presently supply water to the
Cabanatuan Water District (CWD). Five of these were constructed in 1922 and
the others in 1948 and 1961. Water from these wells is pumped directly into
the distributio~ system without treatment. Their total measured discharge

23.       The rated capacities and heads of the pumps are not known.  Because
of the reduced o erating hours of Wells 8, 9 and 10, the daily average supply
is about 5,600 m . Wells 2 to 7 are each provided with diesel standby units
of which only two are in working order. Well No. 3 is not used because the
vertical turbine pump fell in and could not be retrieved.
24.       The distribution system has a ground level and an elevated storage
tank and covers about the same service area as the original 1922 system with
extensions made over the years mostly to the internal network. There is
some 20.6 km of piping ranging from 25 to 150 mm in diameter of which 63 per-
cent is over 50 years old and 76 percent is below 75 mm in 3diameter.  Water
is lifted 28 m from thS ground level storage tank of 300 m capacity to the
elevated tank of 380 m capacity to serve a government building and 15 pri-
vate consumers.
25.       Pressures in the system vary from zero to about 0.5 kgf/cm  1/ and
are sometimes negative making the existing 77 fire hydrants of little value
in fire fighting. These hydrants are mostly inoperable as are the 26 valves
1/   Kilogram force per square centimeter.



ANNEX 7
Page 10
in the system. Because of the prevalent low pressures leakage is estimated
to be about 10% of average water production. In October 1975, there were
4 public faucets and 452 commercial, 19 government connections and one
industrial connection of which only 395 were metered. Most of the meters
were not in good working order.
Deficiencies of the water system
26.       The existing system has many deficiencies.  Although present water
production can supply an average of approximately 260 1/cd to the presently
served population, the majority receive water only in the night because of
leakage and wastage and many of these have installed booster pumps on their
service lines. The principal deficiencies are high water losses due to
leaks in the old distribution pipes, inadequate source capacity and inade-
quate hydraulic capacity in the primary network.
27.       Most of the leaking pipes and services will be replaced under the
project on the basis of a leakage survey and the proposed full metering will
substantially reduce the leakage and wastage. The source capacity will be
increased (see para 38 below) and the hydraulic capacity of the primary net-
work will be improved with adequately sized piping in the internal network,
new main grid transmission pipelines, additional storage and the establish-
ment of pressure zones.
Pressure Zones
28.       Two pressure zones have been proposed in Cabanatuan City, the first
one to be established now for the present service area and the second to
serve high ground in the future. In the new Barrio Pangatian suburb only one
pressure zone is now proposed. The ground elevations for pressure zone I
vary from 25 to 33 m above mean sea level (MSL) and for pressure II the ground
elevations will vary from 33 to 40 m above ISL. Two storage tanks at Bantog
Bulalo and in the Poblacion, each of 1200 m capacity for pressure zone I are
proposed in the Phase I project. The economics of either postponing the
construction of these tanks to the next phase or providing additional wells
for peak demand will be studied during detailed design.
Water Resources
29.       CWD is located near the Central Plain of Luzon, a very productive
groundwater region. The Central Plain is the physiographic expression of a
large structural trough that was depressed below sea level during Tertiary
and perhaps early Quaternary times and filled to its present extent with
alluvium washed down from adjacent mountain slopes. The deepest wells in
the Cabanatuan area are about 200 m deep and penetrate only part of the
Quarternary alluvium which overlies older rocks.



ANNEX 7
Page 11
Aquif er
30.       Within the projected service area of the CWD, the Quaternary
alluvium contains a very productive artesian aquifer. The aquifer consists
of lenses of water-bearing material ranging from fine sands to gravels de-
posited in a complex and random pattern and confined by equally complex
clay lenses. In Cabanatuan the well logs indicate clay at the surface
which inhibits recharge in this area. Recharge to this aquifer is through
infiltration from direct precipitation into the exposed permeable beds and
stream channels along the edge of the foothills east of Cabanatuan. Some
recharge also appears to be from Pampanga River which flows at or above
the piezometric surface of the confined aquifer in the Cabanatuan area.
The sands and gravels of the river channel which extend through the con-
fining surface clay may provide a recharge channel. The groundwater flow
is to the southwest at a gradient of 0.001 to 0.0015 according to a report
on the "Water Resources of Central Luzon" made by the Water Resources Plan-
ning Committee. The a erage transmissivity (T) of the aquifer is estimated
in the report as 400 m /day/m and this value was adopted by the consultants
in the preliminary well designs. The T value is confirmed by data from
wells in the vicinity of Cabanatuan and also in the Tarlac area, 40 km from
Cabanatuan, where the same aquifer appears to have similar characteristics.
Wells
31.       Four of the nine CWD wells, for none of which construction or
performance data are available, produce over 10 1/sec indicating that good
wells can be constructed in this area. Data on well performance is limited
to 38 poorly constructed wells by the Bureau of Public Works (BPW) and two
wells of relatively good construction by the National Irrigation Authority
(NIA). These wells have average specific capacities of 1.3 1/sec/m and
3.9 1/sec/m respectively.
32.  3    Assuming zero recharge, average aquifer transmissivity T of
400 m /day/m average storage coefficient of 0.0001 1/ and 15 wells located
at a spacing of 1 to 2 km in the CWD service area, and each well of 30 1/sec
capacity, it is estimated by the consultants that the total drawdown in the
city center for groundwater withdrawal to meet demands up to year 1990 will
be about 53 m, and about 79 m to meet demands up to year 2000. As several
assumptions are made in the calculation, this drawdown is only indicative
and continuous monitoring of existing and future CWD, BPW and NIA wells
will be needed. If there is extensive uncontrolled development of ground-
water for irrigation around the city the drawdown could be greater. On the
other hand the groundwater regime could possibly stabilise before reaching
the above water levels. CWD should immediately apply to the National Water
Resources Commission (NWRC) for the municipal water rights for at least up
to year 2000 and thereby ensure this economical source.
1/   Assumed by the consultants after examination of aquifer material
reported in the well-logs.

Alternative sources
33.       The principal surface water source in the vicinity of the CWD is
the Pampanga River. This river originates in the Sierra Madre Mountains
located to the east of Cabanatuan and flows in a generally southwesterly
direction, passing along the northern and western periphery of Cabanatuan and
ultimately flowing into Manila Bay. This river and its many tributaries are
the sources of a very extensive NIA irrigation system which serves the major-
ity of the agricultural land from 30 km north of Cabanatuan down to Manila
Bay, 100 km to the south of Cabanatuan.
34.       At present, there are six existing NIA diversion dams within 30 km
of Cabanatuan, and three others to be constructed in the near future. Angat
Dam and the Pantabangan Dam are the only NIA storage dams within the Pampanga
River basin at present, both of which are more than 40 km from Cabanatuan.
35.       Continuous flow records for the Pampanga River at a point approx-
imately three kilometers north of the poblacion of Cabanatuan, exist for the
period from 1964 to 1973.3 Minimum flows during this 10-year period of record
range from 1.21 million m on June 21, 1964, to zero on March 27, 1973. It
would, therefore, be necessary to construct a dam and storage facilities, and
the required transmission pipelines, with treatment and pumping facilities in
order to assure an adequate water supply to meet the future needs of CWD. A
surface water supply from the Pampanga River would therefore present a project
of relatively greater technical complexity and higher cost than the construc-
tion of deep wells.
36.       The banks of the Pampanga River may be suitable for the construc-
tion of induced infiltration wells but because of the unreliability of the
river to provide a full supply during periods of low flow such wells are of
limited value. In the future when the CWD distribution system approaches
the banks of the river, less expensive shallow infiltration wells could pro-
vide service to the adjoining areas and also minimize withdrawals from the
artesian aquifer. Meanwhile accurate information on dry season flows re-
quires to be obtained to aid future planning.
37.       Chemical analyses of samples taken from the nine existing CWD
wells and the two existing NIA wells indicate that these well waters fall
within the maximum allowable limits of the Philippine National Standards for
drinking water. Water from the projected new wells can be expected to be
equally good. The only treatment required would be chlorination to obtain
a residual of 0.02 mg/l 1/.

New Suburb in Barrio Pangatian
40.       Population from Cabanatuan will occupy this suburb situated about
9 km from the city center. In the Phase I development one well and a stor-
age tank will be constructed and distribution mains will be provided to
serve about 800 3housing lots.  The preliminary sizing of the pumps and stor-
age tank (250 m ) is based on the same criteria as for the main city scheme.
Provision is also made for additional service connections for families now
sharing water supply facilities in Cabanatuan moving into this suburb. New
standpipes provided for the lower income families will be paid for by
Cabanatuan Municipality.

D. LIPA WATER DISTRICT
Existing Water Supply Facilities
41.       At present, a group of 14 small springs on Mt. Malepunyo about 8 km
east of Lipa and six deep wells situated within the distribution system, sup-
ply water to Lipa City Water District (LWD). The group of springs, (locally
called the Santo Nino Springs) is located at elevations estimated to range
from 400 m to 825 m on Mt. Malepunyo (summit 963 m). Water is collected in
separate concrete intake boxes which are connected by small pipes to a 6.5 km
long 125 mm diameter cast iron transmission pipeline which delivers water by
gravity to Lipa City. TStal piped flow from those springs as measured in
September 1975 was 880 m /day. Several barrios, along the route of the trans-
mission line from the spring sources, obtain their water supply from the LWD.
42.       The six deep wells within the distribution system Iere measured
in September 1975 to have a combined pumping rate of 2,090 m /day. However,
all pumps are shut down for several hou5s daily, and accordingly, actual
well production is estimated at 1,230 m /day as detailed below:
