43.       The distribution system consists of 12.7 km of piping in diameters
ranging from 25 to 250 mm of which 65 percent is of galvanized steel, 75 mm
diameter or smaller and the rest is of cast iron. There are two storage
facilities, i 760 m  reinforced concrete standpipe filled by Wells No. 1 and
2 and a 40 m storage tank at the Public Market filled by Well No. 5. The
other wells pump directly into the distribution system.



ANNEX 7
Page 15
44.       Pressures in the system vary from zero in tMe higher western and
central parts of the city to an average of 2.8 kgf/cm   1/ in the Santo Nino
transmission pipeline. There are 12 known valves and 40 fire hydrants most
of which are inoperable and of little value in fire fighting. In 1975, there
were 30 public faucets along the Santo Nino transmission pipeline. Most of
these have since been closed down and several private connections given in
that area. As of August 1975, there were 638 domestic, 73 commercial and
3 institutional connections of which only 60 were metered. Most of the
meters were not in good working order.
Deficiencies of the existing system
45.       The present level of service is poor.  The majority of consumers
receive water only a few hours daily even though present water production is
around 230 1/cd. Leakage and wastage is about 65 percent of production. Pipe
carrying capacities in some pipes are seriously reduced by tuberculation.
The storage tanks are seldom cleaned and wells do not have adequate sanitary
protection. Chlorination of the water at Santo Nino Springs and the storage
tanks is intermittent and with variable dosages. Potential cross-connections
between water pipes and polluted drains are numerous and with the low system
pressures constitute a major health hazard. There are no meter and plumbing
shop facilities, and transportation, equipment and spare parts are lacking.
Water resources
46.       LWD is located on a terrace at about 310 m elevation above mean
sea level between the high mountain mass of Mount Malepunyo (and associated
peaks) to the east and Taal Lake at near sea level to the west. This high
terrace slopes gently to lower elevations both north and south from Lipa
City and drops abruptly to the west into Taal Lake. The topographic rela-
tionships are somewhat unfavorable to groundwater development through wells
because recharge is limited. The springs at Mt. Malepunyo which are closer
to Lipa are too small to have any practical application. The larger springs
that are more distant may suffer a decrease in flow with increased ground-
water development (see para 52 below). It may be concluded that the LWD for
the foreseeable future must utilize wells to exploit the underlying aquifers
to their maximum safe yield.
47.       Lipa City lies in a volcanic area.  Taal Lake, is in the crater
of a volcano that erupted recently. This volcano is still active, with
Volcano Island in the lake representing the current crater and center of
volcanic activity. The mountain complex of Mount Malepunyo, Dilanan Hill,
Mount Dalanga and Mount Mataas na Bundoc to the east is a non-active
volcanic area composed of andesitic rock of Quaternary and Late Tertiary
Age. Thirty km farther east, Mount Banahaw is classified as an active
volcano. The section between Mount Malepunyo and Taal Lake has been ex-
plored to about 160 m depth by several wells. The rocks identified in
logs are largely adobe (tuff), tuffaceous and sandstone and clay, all
2
1/   1 kgf /cm   = 10 meters head of water.



ANNEX 7
Page 16
derived from volcanic parent material. Correlation of individual rock units
from well to well is unreliable or virtually impossible, probably in part be-
cause of inconsistent logging nomenclature and lenticular and discontinuous
beds.
Aquifers
48.       The volcanic rocks of the Mount Malepunyo complex comprise an
aquifer that supplied the high springs in the eastern part of Lipa City. The
springs are numerous, but none on the Lipa City side are of large capacity.
However, infiltration in the volcanic mountains not only supplies the springs
but is a source of recharge to the sedimentary aquifers to the west. The
sedimentary aquifer system lying between the mountains and Taal Lake consists
of permeable beds separated by impermeable beds but with no continuous pat-
terns that can be recognized through a study of the available logs of exist-
ing wells. The individual aquifers accept a large amount of recharge from
the hills and mountains to the east, and discharge it in the form of springs
and seeps on the steep escarpment of Taal Lake to the west and on the gentle
slopes to the north and south. The fact that continuity of permeable zones
and impermeable zones exists from recharge area to outcrop area is evident
from a study of piezometric head levels at various depths. For example,
water can be heard falling from a higher aquifer to a lower aquifer in a
test well in Lipa City, and local drillers report that wells drilled below
60 and 90 m have much lower static water levels than shallower wells.
49.       It is rather unusual for deeper aquifers to naturally have much
lower piezometric levels than the shallower aquifers. The implications of
this situation are three-fold. Firstly, the deeper wells will require
greater pumping lifts than shallower wells. Secondly, it is undesirable to
tap two aquifers with the same well because the pumping level would be below
the upper aquifer and water would fall freely in the well bore, entraining
air. Thirdly, if a well taps two aquifers the water from the upper aquifer
will continue to cascade down the bore during non-pumping conditions and so
will deplete the upper aquifer uselessly. All wells drilled below the upper-
most aquifer must therefore be carefully sealed where they pass through
aquifers above the production zone.
Wells
50.       LWD has six operating wells with production capaciSies of 2.6 to
5.8 1/sec which can produce a total of approximately 2,091 m /day. Fernando
Air Base, four kilometers west of 3the poblacion, has eight operating wells
currently producing about 2,180 m /day. The production of the other numerous
wells in the area is not known but with one notable exception discussed below
is probably much less.
51.       The consultants studied information on 58 existing wells and
drilled one test well in Lipa City. The studied wells ranged from 21 to
162 m deep and had specific capacities varying from 0.1 to 3.6 1/sec/m. Well
construction generally is poor and so the better wells are considered more
indicative of general aquifer characteristics than the poorer ones. The



ANNEX 7
Page 17
test well was drilled in an attempt to select a design rate for water pro-
duction. The well was drilled to about 91 m with 250 mm diameter casing,
a slotted pipe screen and gravel packing. The pumping test gave a short
term specific capacity of only 0.5 1/sec/m resulting from a pumping rate of
3.8 1/sec and a drawdown of 8.1 m. Additional test pumping will therefore
be needed before detailed design. A 200 m deep well with a pumping water
level of 76 m and an exceptional production capacity of 32 1/sec was drilled
recently at a site near Fernando Air Base in Lipa. For the preliminary de-
sign the consultants have on the3basis of the limited knowledge assumed an
average well capacity of 1,350 m /day (15.8 1/sec) and that approximately
50 percent of all future wells will extend only to the shallow aquifers, with
a total depth of 100 m and that the balance will extend to a total depth of
200 m, all with a nominal casing diameter of 200 mm.
Springs
52.       The large scale development of groundwater would possibly (1) re-
duce the flow from springs and seeps in the mountains and (2) intercept the
groundwater that now flows from the mountains to the springs below Lipa near
Taal Lake. None of these springs can be economically incorporated into the
LCWD System, but the effects of the groundwater development on Santo Nino
Springs will require to be studied by regular monitoring of flows.
Taal Lake
53.       Taal Lake is a large source of surface water about 9 km distant
and situated as it is 300 m lower than Lipa City poblacion would require a
high pumping lift for its development. Taal volcano is currently active and
the water quality though now within acceptable limits may suffer a future
increase in chlorides from the 428 mg/l 1/ measured in 1975, as a result of
the ongoing volcanic activity. As desalination may be needed in the future
the lake cannot be considered to be an economic source for present develop-
ment.

proposed for Pressure Zone No. 1 and two each in the other pressure zones.
The Public Market well and the Santo Nino springs will be retained to also
serve Pressure Zone No. 1. The water demand in the project period is as

57.        LPTWD now uses only 8,450 m3 /day of the May-it spring discharge
and all of the water from the other five springs. Including the 3,370 m /day
of partially treated water from the DumIaca River facilities, current water pro-
duction is estimated to total 20,140 m /day. The spring intakes typically
consist of reinforced concrete boxes to which are attached the transmission
pipelines. The water flows by gravity to Tayabas (190 m above mean sea
level) and Lucena and Pagbilao (approximately 10 m above mean sea level).
58.        The Dumaca River source facilities consist of an intake, filter
plant and pumping station at Tongko about half way between Tayabas and
Lucena. The filtered water is lifted into an open ground level tank from
which it flows by gravity to Lucena.



ANNEX 7
Page 20
59.        The river intake consists of a short 600 mm diameter pipe which
conveys raw water by gravity direct from the river to the filter which is of
unconventional design having elements common to both rapid sand filters an
slow sand filters. hf the present rate of pump dischyge of about 3,370 m /
day is equated to the filtration rate, then te 88 m single bay filter would
have a corresponding filtration rate of 3.8 m Id/in 1/. This indicates that
the performance is closer to that of a slow sand filter. There, however,
exists a defective backwash system which consists of two open-jointed, 200 mm
pipes (without laterals) extending about 1/3 the length of the sand bed.
Backwash water is pumped from the river, and there are no waste washwater
troughs. Since the backwash system became defective, the bed has been cleaned
infrequently by stirring with a pressure hose stream. This method of cleaning
the bed taken with the absence of inlet baffling and the resulting scouring
action has caused a variation in the sand bed thickness from about 0.5 to
1.0 m. Also, fines have penetrated throughout the depth of the 750 mm filter
media, and a mud blanket varying from about 10 mm thick (inlet end) to 100 mm
thick (far end) has accumulated on top of the filter. The underdrain system
(which is not connected with the backwash system) consists of one 600 mm
perforated and open-jointed pipe without laterals and extending about 1/3 the
length of the filter in a gravel blanket. The filter media are fine sand
from a local beach. The filter can be drained by gravity only to the top of
the media. The walls of the filter and clear-water well leak. This is an
unsatisfactory source because of the poor quality of the treated water.
60.        There are two pumping units driven by 50 and 60 horsepower motors
lifting water from the clear water well to the ground level tank. One of the
two pumping units is currently operated with the discharge valve throttled
to prevent breaking suction. The effective wire-to-water efficiency of this
pumping unit is reported to be 59 percent (efficiency was reduced by 10 per-
cent by the throttled valve). Breakdown of the pumping units is frequent.
61.        Chlorine is applied at six points:  Lab  Grande Spring, Dapdap
Springs, May-it Springs, the Tongko storage facilities and the storage tank
between Tayabas and Pagbilao. The chlorine which is contained in an un-
regulated tank (with simple needle valves) passes through a rubber hose
and mixes with the water at the intake box.
62.        For lack of a suitable master plan, the present LPTWD transmis-
sion system is a complex network of multiple, interconnected mains which
vary in size from 62 to 300 mm and has a total length of about 71.5 km.
About 60 percent of the transmission mains are 150 mm or smaller in diameter
and mostly of unlined cast iron. Recent mains are of asbestos cement. The
system has three storage facilities. Two of these are located at Tongko
along the transmission mains between Tayabas and Lucena near the treatment
plant. The third is located along the transmission mains from Tayabas to
Pagbilao. The two storage tanks at Barrio Tongko are open-top ground tanks

with rii elevations of 70 m above MSL. The smalle5 tank has a capacity of
1,300 m and the larger tank a capacity of 1,900 m . These two tanks receive
all the water pumped from the nearby Tongko filter plant and a portion of the
spring water transmitted to Lucena.  The third storage tank is a dome-covsred
ground tank on the road from Tayabas to Pagbilao with a capacity of 300 m
and an overflow elevation of 76 m above MSL. This tank receives water only
from May-it Spring.
63.        Based on the best information available, total distribution pipes
in the three communities (25.8 km) are divided into 12.5 km in Lucena, 5.5 km
in Tayabas and 7.8 km in Pagbilao. Sixty-one percent of all distribution
piping is smaller than 100 mm in diameter; 67 percent is of galvanized steel;
and 90 percent has been place for over 45 years.  Pipes 100 mm and larger in
diameter are of unlined cast iron.  Pressures in t?e Lucena distribution
system are variable ranging from zero to 2 kg f/cm  because of water ration-
ing.  In Tayabas and Pagbilao the upper end of the range is only 0.8 kg f/cm
because of inadequate distribution system piping.
64.        Information on the number and location of valves is lacking.
There are a total of 84 hydrants, of which 49 are in Lucena, 18 in Tayabas
and 17 in Pagbilao. Most hydrants are inoperable and have pressures too
low for fire-fighting. There are a total of 83 public faucets of which
19 are in Lucena, 18 in Tayabas, and 21 in Pagbilao. Twenty-five public
faucets are installed along transmission mains, mostly between Tayabas and
Lucena and between Tayabas and Pagbilao. Most of the public faucets within
the distribution systems are attached to fire hydrants. In August 1975 there
were 4,600 domestic, 656 commercial, 55 industrial and 3 institutional serv-
ice connections in the water district and only 22 connections with function-
ing meters.
Deficiencies of the existing system
65.        The design and operation of the existing LPTWD water supply
facilities are inadequate and inefficient. The masonry top of the May-it
Spring intake box is not water-tight. A cross-connection exists between the
Dapdap intake boxes and an irrigation ditch. The Dumacaa River diversion
consists of loosely scattered rocks. It has no screening, allowing debris
to enter the filter bed. The quality of treated water from the filter is
variable. Carrying capacities of most of the cast iron and galvanized steel
pipes have been seriously reduced by tuberculation. Leakage in mains and
service pipes is excessive, and repair is usually makeshift because of poor
workmanship and the lack of spare parts.
66.        Many of the public faucets are left open, wasting water continually,
and several of the valves that can be located are inoperative. Most consumers
in Pagbilao have water service only for two or three hours daily. Service is
intermittent over most of the Lucena distribution system. Many consumers
in Lucena and Pagbilao have to use booster pumps on their service lines in
order to obtain water. Several consumers have recently requested to be



ANNEX 7
Page 22
disconnected from the water system because of the unavailability of water
at their connections, even though water obtained from present supply sources
is equivalent to approximately 400 1/cd for present served population.
Consumers sometimes operate distribution valves in order to increase pressures
and water availability in their homes. Private plumbing is not regulated, and
private plumbers take unwarranted liberties in tapping the water distribu.ion
system. Potential cross-connections between water pipes and polluted water
are numerous, with many pipes which are laid in drains being subjected daily
to zero (sometimes negative) pressures.
67.        The LPTWD lacks the basic tools and equipment for proper mainte-
nance of the system. There are no stocks of materials to be used for repair.
Parts needed for repair are often taken from one part of the system to
repair another. There is only one semi-operative four-passenger jeep for
transportation. There are no meter or plumbing shop facilities. Plans of
the existing LPTWD system are incomplete and inaccurate.
Water Resources
68.        LPTWD is located on the southeast flank of a recently active
volcano, Mount Banahaw, and extends down the slope to the sea at Tayabas Bay.
This flank of Mount Banahaw is prolific in large springs, which provide
the major portion of the present LPTWD supply. Wells of small to moderate
capacity provide almost all the water for private and commercial supplies
and also for some governmental agencies and systems that do not use LPTWD
water.
Aquifers
69.        Most of the rocks underlying the LPTWD are the flows, pyroclastics
and volcanic debris that form the slope of Mount Banahaw. Inter-fingering
with these volcanic rocks are Quaternary and Late Tertiary marine and terres-
tial clastic sediments (gravels, sands and clays) which are exposed between
Pagbilao and Tayabas. A narrow coastal strip is covered by alluvium.
70.        The known and potentially good aquifers in the LPTWD areas are the
volcanics on the mountain flanks, which feed the high springs, and the sands
and gravels which supply most of the well production. The volcanic aquifer
is very productive as evidenced by the springs on Mount Banahaw, but no tests
or detailed investigations have been made to determine aquifer parameters,
and no significant records on long-term spring water flow have been kept.
Recharge from rainfall, on Mount Banahaw is significant, although it cannot
be quantified because of the lack of existing data.
Wells
71.        Numerous wells have been drilled in the LPTWD area to provide
the major water supply for residents, commercial enterprises and government
agencies not served by LPTWD. Records and data for 33 wells of varying
reliability and detail were obtained by the consultants to evaluate the
groundwater resources. The two deepest wells in the area are reportedly



ANNEX 7
Page 23
over 600 m deep and located at Pagbilao and at Lucena. Both wells are
moderately productive but the water is of undesirable chemical quality,
one of them reported to be saline. The most productive well known in the
area, with a yield of 18.9 1/sec, is 122 m deep and located at the Tongko
storage tank site. However, the water is high in iron content and the
well has been abandoned.
72.        Static water levels of two to 20 m with specific capacities
of 0.8 1/sec/m or less are common. This is low because the existing wells
are constructed with poor materials and techniques and do not tap the
entire effective aquifer. It would appear that most wells constructed
in the Lucena area, given good techniques (electric logging and gravel
packing) and materials (continuous wire-wrapped screen), should be capable
of producing 10 to 25 1/sec although care would be required to avoid pro-
ducing the saline water that seems to occur at depth. In the Pagbilao
area however wells tend to be less productive and with poor water quality.
Much more study and a test well program will therefore be necessary before
wells are considered to be a feasible alternative and even so the possibility
of need for treatment makes this less attractive than other sources.
Surface Water Sources
73.        The major surface water sources in the study area are the Dumacaa
and Iyam Rivers. The minimum flows 2of the Dumacaa River, which has an
approximate drainage area of 100 km , are currently being utilized by existing
NIA irrigatio~ projects. The lyam river, which has an approximate drainage
area of 90 km , is fed by many small tributaries having long shallow channels
and have no topographically feasible site for impoundment. The waters of
either of these rivers, if used as municipal water sources would require
treatment. When compared with groundwater sources, development of surface
water sources is considerably more costly, including the capital and annual
costs of a dam, water treatment plant and pump station. Furthermore, practical
difficulties related to conflict with potential irrigation water user rights
may be anticipated. Therefore, no further consideration is given to surface
water sources.
Spring Sources
74.        The total measured flows from the 15 major springs near Tayabas
is 92,369 m /day (see para 56) far in excess of the 1985 average demand of
13,000 m /day and more than double year 2000 LPTWD average demand of 39,100
m /day estimated by the consultants. The springs are located to the west and
northwest of Tayabas within a distance of about 5 km. Because of the good
water quality, which results in treatment economies and since there will be
no pumping with consequent energy savings, the springs present the most
economical alternative for LPTWD. Therefore, the consultants proceeded on
the study of the most economical combination of spring sources to meet long
term needs. This was done by making present worth analyses and the chosen
alternative represents the least cost solution.



ANNEX 7
Page 24
F. SAN FERNANDO (LA UNION) WATER DISTRICT
Existing Water Supply Facilities
75.        The San Fernando (La Union) Water District (LUWD) is made up of the
towns of San Fernando, San Juan, Bauang, Bacnotan and San Gabriel. The latter
three cities do not have piped water supply systems. San Fernando and San Juan
obtain their water supply by gravity from two sources. The main source, pro-
viding 90% of the total supply is a diversion dam at elevation 280 m above
mean sea level (MSL) on the Lon-oy River, about 16 km from San Juan and 10 km
east of San Gabriel   The minimum recorded flow at the dam site in the period
1954-57 was 5,100 m /day of which about 4000 m /day is diverted for use by San
Fernando and San Juan. Seven small springs at Cadaclan, some 5 to 8 km east
of San Fernindo at elevations varying from 100 to 200 m above MSL, provide
about 300 m /day for San Fernando.
76.        An 11.6 km transmission pipeline of cast iron reducing from 250 mm
to 200 mm in diameter followed by a 150 mm diameter asbestos 5ement pipe
conveys the water from the Lon-oy diversion works to a 2800 m sedimentation
tank at elevation 115 m above MSL near San Gabriel. The sedimentation tank
has alum solution mixing and feeding facilities which are used about 30 days
in a year when turbidity is high. Chlorine is applied continuously by
gravity hose to the outlet pipe.
77.        Small dams at the Cadaclan springs divert the water into galvanized
steel pipes varying from 32 to 75 mm in diameter, totaling about 12 km and
conveyging to a 3.5 km long 150 mm diameter transmission pipeline leading to a
450 m storage tank at an elevation of 59 m above MSL in San Fernando. This
tank is also connected to the San Gabriel sedimentation tank by an 18.0 km
pipeline of cast iron (300 mm and 250 mm diameter) and asbestos (200 3mm
diameter). This pipeline is also connected near San Juan to a 150 m storage
tank at elevation 66 m above MSL to serve the town. Because of a defective
self closing inlet valve this tank is bypassed by a 25 mm diameter connection
from the pipeline to the San Juan distribution system. The hydraulic gradient
at this point is 90 m above MSL.
78.        At present there are a total of 17.9 km of distribution piping -
14.6 km in San Fernando and 3.3 km in San Juan. Of this total 28% is
galvanized steel piping which has been in place for 50 years. About 25%
of the piping is below 100 mm in diameter and 22% above that size. About 7.2
km of the total 7.4 km of 100 mm pipe is of asbestos cement. Available records
indicate there are 19 fire hydrants and 22 valves in San Fernando but no fire
hydrants and 9 valves in San Juan. There are no public faucets in either
town.



ANNEX 7
Page 25
Deficiencies of the existing system
79.        The present level of service to San Fernando and San Juan is
very low mostly because of poor design and inefficient operation of the
system. About one-third of the consumers receive water only about four hours
daily. Though pressures are generally good, leakage from the system is
correspondingly high. In San Fernando some 3.2 km of new pipes were laid
in parallel to leaking older pipes, but not all of the service connections
have been transferred to the new system. Most of the valves are lost and
there are no accurate plans of the system.
80.        There are no air relief valves on some 30 peaks in the main from
Lon-oy to the San Gabriel sedimentation tank. As constructed, this main is
subject to frequent wash out and blow outs. The main below the San Gabriel
sedimentation basin operates against the high hydraulic gradient of the
San Fernando storage tank. This creates high pressure and increased leakage
along the transmission main and prevents the sedimentation basin being use-
ful for operational storage. The Lon-oy river water though mostly clear
becomes turbid following rains, but the facilities at the sedimentation
basin are inadequate to remove the temporary turbidity.
81.        The Cadaclan transmission system also operates against the
hydraulic gradient of the San Fernando storage tank. Because of leakage
and unauthorized use, little water, however, reaches the storage tank and
sometimes there is reverse flow of Lon-oy water into the Cadaclan transmis-
sion pipeline.
Water Demand
82.        On the basis of several assumptions (see Part F, Annex 8) water
production for LUWD in 1985 is calculated to be about 2.8 million m3/year
(250 1/cd) in 1985. The consultants have analyzed the water production for
the five municipalities in the project for 1985, 1990 and 2000 as follows:

83.       The consultants design criteria on the growth of these municipalities
may be overstated. Peak hour demand in San Fernando is unlikely to be 175
percent of average day demand and may be nearer to 150 percent, as most of the
hotels likely to impose this demand are away from the poblacion and have their
own service tanks. The general economy of the area does not support the
findings that with improves service even if average annual water demand may
double, from 1.4 million m to 2.8 million m from 1976 to 195 it appears
unlikely that it would again increase by 70% to 4.8 million m into 1990. For
the purposes of the preliminary engineering studies it is therefore proposed
to design the system mostly to provide water to San Fernando and the resort

area between San Fernando and Bauang. Though extensions will be made in the
other municipalities, peak hour demand will be met up to about 1983. After
that year, additional source development and some improvements in transmission
and storage may be needed to serve San Fernando, if the demand as now projected
comes about. (see para 94 below).
Water Resources
84.        The low lying coastal areas of La Union are covered with recent
alluvium and beach deposits including coralline limestone. Exposed in the
hills near the shore and buried beneath the recent deposits are Quaternary
to Late Tertiary clays, sands and tuffs. At greater depth, and exposed
in mountains farther from shore, are older Tertiary marine sediments over-
lying a basement complex of Early Tertiary to Late Cretaceous intrusives
and metamorphosed volcanics.
Wells
85.        Bacnotan, San Gabriel and Bauang, at present, are almost entirely
dependent on shallow well water; so are most of the residents of San Juan
and San Fernando. In those cases where the aquifer is good (frequently
coralline limestone), water salinity is a problem. If good water can be
obtained from an upper zone in the aquifer, production must be strictly
limited to avoid intrusion of underlying saline water. The sand aquifers
generally seem limited in transmissivity and the large drawdowns associated
with high production rates would encourage saline intrusion. Thus, the
coastal areas do not appear favorable for providing a large-scale groundwater
source for LUWD. In the hills inland of the coast, aquifers are poor and
large-capacity wells suitable for municipal use are also unlikely to be
found. However, several of the larger rivers appear to have suitable gravels
and sands in their channels for the construction of relatively large-
capacity induced infiltration wells.
86.        The channels of the Naguilian and Bauang Rivers at Barrio Namating,
about 2 km east of Bauang, appear to be suitable for induced infiltration
wells. The channel of the Baroro River from San Gabriel to about 5 km down
stream also appears to be suitable for such wells. Three test wells were
drilled by the consultants in these river channels to determine preliminary
aquifer coefficients. The test-well in the Naguilian River channel encoun-
tered clay below 9.1 m depth and was therefore abandoned. The test well in
the Bauang River drilled about 3 km east of Bauang, however, encountered
sands and gravels with some clay throughout to a depth of 30.5 m. A 150 mm
diameter slotted-pipe screen was placed in a sand and gravel section from
22.3 to 27.8 m depth. A 50 mm diameter observation well was drilled 20 m
away to a depth of 27.0 m and screened from 24.3 to 26.4 m. The test pumping
equipment was erratic and the drawdown curve for the well shows large varia-
tions wherever the 19.6 1/sec pumping rate of the flow of the pump was
adjusted, about 5 minutes and 15 minutes after the commencement of the test.
Three more such adjustments were made before the pumping water level stabi-
lized at a drawdown of about 10.8 m after about 1000 minutes. Though water



ANNEX 7
Page 28
levels were taken after pumping stopped about 2 days from commencement,
almost full recovery took place in the first minute and therefore made
Lransmissivity calculations infeasible.  After full recovery from the test
another short pumping test of 25 minutes achieved the same drawdown in about
10 minutes. The observation well drawdow-n curve shows no effect for about 200
minutes but slowly rises about 10 cm, over the pumping period.  No river water
levels were kept, but it is presumed that this rise was caused by a rise in
the river water level. It is, however, clear that there is no hydraulic
connection between the observation well and the pumping well. The specific
capacity of the well after two days of pumping at 19.5 1/sec was 1.8 1/sec/
mieter. A maximum safe pumping rate for this well proposed ay the consultants
is a little more than the test rate, 23.1 I/sec (or 2,000 m /day) with a
pumping level of about 18 m below ground level and four meters above the top
of the screen.
37.        On the basis of this experience and since induced infiltration
Wells are found to be the most economic alternative, the consultants propose a
production well drilling program near the existing test well in Ba ang River
with the expectation that one out of three may not meet the 2000 m /day test
pumping rate standard.  However, with better drilling and development tech-
niques and screen. than those used for the test wells, and 3a design improved
through local experience, higher pumping rates than 2000 m /day may be
achieved.
88.        The test well on the bank of the Baroro River drilled about one
kilometer from San Gabriel encountered an upper section of fine to coarse
sand down to a depth of 6 m, a sandy clay section from 6 m to 24 m, and
another section of fine to coarse sand from 24 m to 36.6 m.  Approximately
9 m of the 200 mm diameter perforated casing was installed in the lower sand
section from 26 to 35 m. A 50 mm diameter observation well was constructed
approximately 15 rm downstream of the test well, on a line parallel to the edge
of the river, intercepting essentially the same strata encountered by the test
well. The drawdown test was made with a pumping rate of 12.6 1/sec for 48
hours with water levels taken during this period and for 17 hours during
tihe recovery. The decrease in the slope of the drawdown curve after 5 to 6
minutes of pumping generally shows recharge from the river. After pumping
stopped the well recovered to within 0.33 in of the original water level in one
nminute and almost to the original level in 10 minutes, making a calculation
of transmissivity from the recovery test curve infeasible.  Assuming that the
aquifer is homogeneous and ignoring boundary conditions the consultants have
calculated iransmissivitv on the basis of the drawdown test to be between
26 and 59 m /day/m.  The observation well had a drawdown of only 0.14 m in the
First day of pumping in creasing, to 0.24 ID at the end of the second day with
a correspondingly insignificant recovery of 0.01 m over 1000 minutes. This
indicates that (as in the case of the Bauang River test well) that there is
no hydraulic connection between the observation well and the pumping well and
Jakes the assumption of homogenity rather doubtful.



ANNEX 7
Page 29
89.        The specific capacity of the well after 48 hours of pumping at a
rate of 12.6 1/sec was found to be 0.85 1/sec/m. The consultants propose a
design pumping rate for this well to be approximately 60 percent of the test
rate, 20.5 l/sec or 1,800 m /day, with the pumping water level near the top of
the screened section. As the conditions here are generally similar to those
in the Bauang River test-well the consultants recommend similar development
techniques as discussed above. The water quality in the test wells is within
acceptable standards. There is danger of industrial pollution of the Bauang
River from the chemical factory a few miles upstream, and assurances should
be obtained by LUWD and LWUA from NWRC that this would be closely controlled.
Surface Water Sources
90.        The LUWD contains several streams flowing into the China Sea,
generally in a westerly direction. Among these, the two major rivers, the
Bauang to the south and Baroro, flowing past San Gabriel to the north can be
considered to be potential water supply sources for the LUWD.
Bauang River. The two major tributaries of the Bauang River are the
Naguilian and Ribusan Rivers. The Naguilian River flows have been
measured at a gauging station, located near Barrio Mamating2 The drain-
age area of the river at the gaging station is about 304 km 3 The
minimum recorded flow from 1947 to 1972 was about 130,000 m /day. This
minimum river flow is substantially greater than the projected maximum
daily demand (35,100 m /day) for LUWD service area in the year 2000.
Baroro River. The Baroro river flows have been measured since 1958
at a gauging station located near Barrio Cabaroan in the municipality
of San Juan. There are two irrigation diversion dams near San Gabriel
poblacion, upstream of the gauging station. The recorded minimum stream
flow at this station is 1,700 m /day. Because of the existing irrigation
diversions, most of this minimum flow can be attributed to the Cabassitan
River, a major tributary, which joins the Baroro River main channel about
one kilometer downstream of San Gabriel Poblacion. The Lon-oy water
intake is located about 16 km upstream from the gaging station at
Cabaroan, on the spring-fed main tributary. If it is technically and
economically feasible to develop the Lon-oy source the 4,000 m /day
g5avity supply could meet the estimated 1985 peak hour demand of 1,800
m /day by San Juan, San Gabriel and Bacnotan and in the interim supple-
ment San Fernanio water supply. The Baroro river, with a minimum flow
of only 1,700 m /day at San Gabriel may be considered as a further
supplementary source with an induced3infiltration well scheme to increase
the supply from the north to 5,800 m /day and after supplying San Juan,
San Gabriel and Bacnotan meet up to 40 percent of San Fernando's peak
hour demand in 1985.



ANNEX 7
Page 30
Springs. The existing Lon-oy source, although locally referred to
as a "spring", is actually a surface water source.  Field investiga-
tions of the headwaters of the Baroro River up to about 3 km upstream
from Barrio Lon-oy were carried out to locate major springs which
could be tapped directly at the source, with the possibility of
eliminating extensive treatment works required for surface water.
Several spring fed tributaries, passing through rugged terrain,
were observed, but no single major spring outlet was located. The
stream flow was a combination of complex and more or less uniformly
distributed seepages along the steep slopes of the river canyon.
Consequently, the Lon-oy in which turbidity increases after rain
must be categorized as a surface water source - requiring improved
intake facilities, a reliable transmission pipeline and better
treatment than now existing. The technical and economic feasibility
of developing this source will require study before detailed design.
The Cadaclan source of the old San Fernando system also consists of
several spring-fed streams formed in a similar manner as the Lon-oy
Sprins. The total measured yield of the Cadaclan source of about
300 m /day is too small to be considered in a long-term water supply
plan.
Water Supply Development Alternatives
91.        The consultants studied a number of alternatives for the devel-
opment of the water resources for meeting LUWD long-term demand and have
recommeVded the construction of 3 induced infiltration wells each of
1,000 m /day capacity in the Baroro River channel as part of the least cost
solution. One alternative not considered in detail is the feasibility
of retaining and improving the existing Lon-oy-San Gabriel gravity scheme,
if not for the long term, at least to meet 1985 needs and until the
induced infiltration well scheme is fully developed.
92.        According to local information obtained in September 1976, water
from the Lon-oy source was turbid for only 28 days in the previous 12
months. One possibility for removing this turbidity could be She construc-
cion of two 1,000 m 1/ slow sand filters to treat the 4,000 m day discharge.
A technical and economic analysis of this alternative compared with pumping
the same quantity from out of induced infiltration wells as recommended
by the consultants will be made before de tailed design. The appraisal
report is based on the assumption that the Lon-oy source will be retained
up to 1985.
93.        For the Baulng source development, 5 wells each of 2,000 m /day
capacity and a 2,000 m storage tank were proposed by the consultants for the
Phase I project. However, because the demand projections are based on
assumptions which can only be verified with time, the number of wells are
reduced to 3 and the storage postponed to the future. A corresponding
reduction is made in the sizing of the transmission pipelines. The effect
of this reduction would be to advance construction of the next phase project
from 1985 to about 1983, if the consultants demand projections are found

G. TARLAC WATER DISTRICT
Existing Water Supply Facilities
97.        Five out of seven existing deep wells supplied water to the Tarlac
Water District (TWD) in 1975. Three of these were constructed in 1928
and the others between 1962 and 1972. One of the two wells that were not in
use was abandoned because of pollution and the other because of various
operatignal problems. The total measured discharge from the wells was
1,575 m /day as shown below.
Motor                   3

98.        As part of the early action program, TWD proposes rehabilitating
the two unused wells in order to improve the water supply. A reinforced
concrete elevated tank adjacent to important government offices was3installed
in 1928 as part of the original system. It has a capacity of 380 m and
overflow elevation of about 72 m above mean sea level. This tank being well
above the operating hydraulic gradient, is valved off from the distribution
system. It is filled by Wells Nos. I and 2 and provides water to nearby
government offices. A diesel-driven booster pump located at the base of the
tank, has been long abandoned.
99.        The distribution system is at present, divided by valve closures
into five service areas: the "old system", the "new system", the area fed by
Well No. 7, the area fed by Well No. 6, and those government offices supplied
by the elevated tank. The majority of TCWD consumers are connected to the
"old system", which is essentially the original 1928 system less some
abandoned pipelines in the downtown area, and less those sections now isolated
and served by Wells Nos. 6 and 7. Wells Nos. 1 and 2 supply water to the "old
system", only between about 6 a.m. to 2 p.m. During the approximately eight
hours when water is provided, except in the immediate vicinity of the wells,
pressures are zero or negative. Consumers use pumps (hand or electric) on
their service lines to obtain water. The "new system" serves the second
largest group of consumers, including many commercial establishments. It
consists of a stretch of 100-mm diameter pipeline laid in 1968 from the
elevated tank along the river in the downtown area. Well No. 4 is now iso-
lated to serve this are on a 24-hour basis. Distribution pressures range
from zero to 0.8 kgf/cm , and water is generally available. Wells Nos. 6 and
7 serve short segments of 75 mm pipe on San Vicente Road and Camp Makabulos
where pressures are very low, but water is generally available.



ANNEX 7
Page 33
100.       There is approximately 19 km of distribution piping in the system.
Seventy percent (70%) of the piping is 75 mm or smaller diameter GS pipes
which have been in place for almost 50 years, and the rest is cast iron pipe
of diameters ranging from 100 mm to 200 mm.
Deficiencies of the Water System
101.       The TWD water system has many deficiencies similar to those in the
neighboring CWD. The majority of consumers receive water only a few hours
daily. Many consumers have been disconnected, and several blocks of distribu-
tion main have been abandoned, because of water shortage. Fire-fighting
capabilities are practically non-existent. The above is true even though
present water production is equivalent to approximately 309 1/cd for the
presently served population.
102.       System design and operation are inefficient.  Small-diameter
discharge pipes on Wells Nos. 4 and 6 greatly reduce production from those
wells. The elevated tank is filled from an overhead line which requires a
relatively higher hydraulic grade line in the system. Wells Nos. 1 and 2.
which are the only sources of water for the majority of consumers, are
valved off from the distribution system for some 16 hours daily to fill the
elevated tank, which in turn is valved off from the distribution system.
This occurs even though there is sufficient existing well capacity to refill
the tank during minimum-use night hours. Potential cross-connections between
water pipes and polluted water are numerous, with many pipes (often with under
zero or negative pressures) laid in drains. The elevated tank is seldom
cleaned, wells do not have adequate sanitary protection and the water supply
receives no treatment. There are no water quality laboratory facilities,
and no routine water sampling and testing program. Some consumers have
complained of "foul" water.  There is a shortage of equipment, tools and
spare parts. The district has no motor vehicle for transportation. There
are no plumbing and meter shop facilities.
Pressure Zones
103.       The greater part of the water district lies at ground elevations
of 30 m to 50 m above mean sea level with only the area southwest of Tarlac
between the National Road and the Tarlac River ranging from 50 m to 70 m.
The area lower than the 50 m contour with the highest water demand, can be
served from an hydraulic grade line (HGL) of 70 m. The area above the 50 m
contour can be served from an HGL of 90 m. By designating two pressure
zones, the water district could reduce the pumping heads over the area with
the highest water demand and thereby reduce operating costs. This preliminary
conclusion by the consultants requires to be reviewed on the basis of an eco-
nomic comparison between providing storage and/or drilling additional wells
to meet peak hour demand. The comparison will be made at the time of detailed
design.
Water Resources
104.       The TWD is located on the western edge of the Central Plain of
Luzon and the present service area, as well as almost all of the projected



ANNEX 7
Page 34
service area, lies in a very productive groundwater region. Wells are almost
universally used for municipal, private and industrial water supply and are
also currently used in a large-scale private irrigation project (Tarlac
Development Corporation's Luisita Project). Wells are also being studied as
potential sources for a very large-scale National Irrigation Administration
(NIA) project to supplement surface water. The Central Plain of Luzon is the
physiographic expression of a large structural trough separating the Zambales
Mountains to the west from the Sierra Madres to the east. This trough was
depressed below sea level during late Tertiary and perhaps early Quaternary
times. The trough was filled to its present extent with material washed down
from the mountain slopes and deposited in the form of fan and deltaic deposits,
and later, flood plain deposits. The deepest wells in the Tarlac area are
about 300 m deep and penetrate only part of the Quarternary alluvium.
Aquifer
105.       Tarlac and Cabanatuan obtain their water supplies from the same
aquifer system, which is described in paragraph 30 above. There are 13 NIA
wells and 10 Luisita project wells for which reliable specific capacity data
are available. The specific capacities of these 23 wells average 3.85 1/sec/m
and the storage coefficient for one of the NIA tests wells is reported3to be
0.0000826. From this data, an average aquifer transmissivity of 450 m /day/m
and storage coefficient of about 0.0001 (a typical artesian aquifer figure)
has been assumed by the consultants. Though this is adequate for the pre-
liminary engineering studies, actual well design should be based upon test
pumping and good well development. As the aquifer is not homogeneous and
isotropic the aquifer coefficients assumed by the consultants are only
indicative. This is confirmed by the variation in specific capacities
of wells near the Tarlac poblacion being below 4.0 1/sec/m as compared with
wells outside the poblacion.
Wells
106.       A general design for an efficient production well for Tarlac has
been developed by the consultants from available data. For the greatest effi-
ciency, so as to avoid excessive drawdown and minimize costs, it is recommended
that these wells are drilled to Lhe NIA standards. For the same reasons, all
stratigraphic zones that are indicated to be productive by electric and stra-
tigraphic logs should be screened because in an anisotropic aquifer such as
in Tarlac, the specific capacity will increase greatly as the percentage
of total permeable screened section increases. It is anticipated that such
wells should produce 44 1/sec at between 10 and 30 m of initial drawdown in
the area near Tarlac poblacion and other areas where the specific capacity of
existing wells is indicated as poor (less than 4.0 1/sec/m). These same
wells should produce 63 I/sec at between 10 and 15 m of initial drawdown in
the areas where specific capacity of wells is indicated as good (over 4.0
1/sec/m).
107.       A well situated near the river bank encountered seven meters of
sand at the surface, and therefore induced infiltration wells along the Tarlac
River as a feasible alternative was also considered. However, the NIA diverts



ANNEX 7
Page 35
the total flow of the river at a site far above Tarlac for irrigation use
for several months each year. This renders induced infiltration wells
impractical. The recommended drilling program consists of four production
wells of 44 1/sec capacity spaced one to two kilometers apart in the
existing TWD service area. These wells should be pumped intensively for
several years and the aquifer response monitored. The data can then be
interpreted to serve as a guide to further development of the well system
which probably will consist of 44 and 63 1/sec wells spaced about two
kilometers apart.
108.       Each production well should have facilities for measuring quantity
of production or rate of production, times of operation, and water levels.
Routine monthly observations of static and pumping water levels should be
recorded and daily records of pumping be kept. Water samples should be
collected for bacterial analysis monthly and for chemical analysis annually.
It would also be desirable to monitor static water levels in several obser-
vation wells located within the area spanned by TWD wells but far enough
from any well to minimize local drawdown effects. Similar routine static
water level measurements should be made in observation wells surrounding
the well field at a distance.
109.       The water that would be produced from new TWD deep wells is
expected to be satisfactory in all respects except possibly for marginal
iron content. There has been no complaint recorded in the water district
about the iron content at the present levels. Complaints about "brown
water" are usually due to corrosion in the existing unlined cast iron or
steel pipelines.
Water Demand
110.       Two of the elisting five deep wells, Wells Nos. 4 and 6 with a
total capacity of 840 m /day (9.7 1/sec) are proposed for standby duty. The
total installed capacity on completion of the four 44 1/sec wells will there-
fore be about 186 I/sec. As can be seen from the table of water demand up
to 1985 summarized below, the wells should be able to meet peak hour demand
up to 1983 without any storage. Before detailed design the economic feasi-
bility of postponing or entirely doing away with storage will be examined.

PHILIPPINES
PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Population and Water Demand Projections
A. INTRODUCTION
Population
1.        The Master Plans for the six cities have population and water demand
projections up to the year 2000. Past population trends were calculated from
the 1948, 1960 and 1970 national census data for all of the barrios (political
subdivisions) in the present service areas of each Water District (WD).
Future service areas have been defined by the WDs with the help of the consul-
tants on the basis of available information on land use and urban planning
data for the years 1990 and 2000.
2.        On the basis of past population trends, future growth rates have been
assumed according to the particular circumstances of each WD. Because of in-
sufficient local data, high and low growth assumptions were not possible.
The main assumption made in the consultants' projections for most of the WDs
is that growth rates from 1970 to 1980 will be about the same as in the pre-
vious 10 years after which there will be a small decline. In the previous
10 years the country as a whole had a high annual growth rate of about 3% - a
result of high fertility and low mortality - but the growth rate in the pro-
vinces were even higher. As the on-going national family planning programs
will take longer in gaining acceptance in the provinces where literacy is low,
the conservative assumption made by the consultants is acceptable for planning
purposes. Each WD has a somewhat different population pattern, e.g, Baguio
and La Union have transient populations in the summer months and Tarlac had a
growth rate of only 2.5% in recent years.
Water Demand
3.        With the exception of Baguio most of the other WDs were only par-
tially metered at the time the consultants made the feasibility study. The
accuracy of the meters and the meter readings in all of the WDs is very low.
For example, in Cabanatuan3an analysis of 360 metered domestic Sonnections
gave a consumption of 33 m /month/connection compared with 23 m /month in the
neighboring city of Tarlac having similar distribution system pressures and
socio-economic conditions. Similar difficulties were met in the other WDs in
the project in the collection of reliable data. As the project provides for
extending piped water service to the lower income groups only modest increases
in water consumption are anticipated. The consultants' assumptions will be
reviewed after project completion and during the annual rate reviews discussed
in para 6.15 of the appraisal report.



ANNEX 8
Page 2
4.        In the last few months all the WDs have started on a program for
replacing defective meters and the installation of new meters with the objec-
tive of full metering of all service connections. By the time of the start
of detailed design in early 1977 this program should be well advanced in most
of the WDs and the design consultants will have more accurate information for
revising the demand projections.
B. BAGUIO WATER DISTRICT
Service Area
5.        Baguio City covers an area of approximately 4900 ha of which half is
developed. Within the city boundaries Baguio Water District (BWD) presently
serves an area of about 1,900 ha including the central business district
(poblacion) and portions of 12 adjacent barrios. The project will provide
improvements and extensions to the present service area. For the long term
the service area is expected to increase in size to about 2,200 ha by 1990
and 2,600 ha by the year 2000.
Population Projections
6.        The resident population in Baguio City increased at the rate of 4.6%
between 1948 and 160 and 5.3% between 1960 and 1970. The city carried out a
census in 1975 and found that because of the slow down in the economy the
growth rate in that period was only 2.6%. It is now expected that with the
improving economic situation the high growth will be resumed between 1975 and
1980 at 4.5%, which is somewhat less than the 5.3% experienced in the past
decade. After 1980 the growth rate is expected to further decline to about
3.9%.
7.        In addition to the "permanent" resident population there is a
"'transient" (floating) population of students and summer vacationists.
Education is a major industry in Baguio and during the 9 months school year
the student population according to the 1970 census, was almost 28% of the
total population living in city. During the summer months about half of
this student population leaves the city to be replaced by vacationists. In
the peak of the summer holiday season of 1970, vacationists were estimated to
be 30% of the total population living in city. The statistics on vacationists
are however unreliable as only an annual total count of people entering the
city is kept and the consultants had to make several assumptions in estimating
the peak numbers. Projections of future student populations and vacationists
assume a growth rate of 3% which is the same as the national growth rate.
Population Served
8.        In 1970 only about 82% of the permanent resident population in the
city lived within the BWD service area. By 1985 this is projected to in-
crease to 88%. The population served is calculated on the basis of surveys
made of typical households and is expected to reduce from 8.2 persons per
connection in 1976 to 7.5 persons per connection in 1985.

Water Consumption
10.       With the gradual implementation of the metering program in Baguio,
accuracy of the statistics on water consumption is constantly being improved.
Based on recent surveys the consultants estimated average resident domestic
consumption to be 98.7 1/cd. The mission, however, found that with new infor-
mation from the metering program and recent calibrations of the discharge
from tubewells this to be about 71 1/cd, significantly lower than average
consumption in the other cities in the project and is attributed to Baguio's
much cooler climate and higher rainfall. Transient students and vacationists
are estimated to consume one-third less, 46 1/cd.
11.       Following the completion of an early action program in 1977 there is
expected to be a sharp increase in permanent domestic consumption to 81 1/cd
followed by smaller increases until 1981, to 90 1/cd when the project becomes
operational and then to 110 1/cd by 1985. Transient domestic consumption is
expected to increase to 56 1/cd over these years. Commercial, industrial and
institutional consumption including wholesale by BWD is estimated at 25% of
domestic consumption throughout the project period.
Unaccounted for Water
12.       Unaccounted for water is now a high 60%.  Some of this is because
of poor management in meter reading and possibly unmetered usage. Most of
it is due to losses from the long transmission pipeline and the distribution
network caused by the high pressures due to the extreme variations in eleva-
tion in the city. Because of the great age of the network (see Annex 7,
Section B) and the frequency of burst pipes, an estimation of the leakage
through a survey was not considered necessary by the consultants, who have
recommended replacing most of the old pipes. It is assumed that unccounted
for water will be reduced by half by about 1982 and to one quarter by 1985.
As about one-third of the project cost is in the replacement of old pipelines,



ANNEX 8
Page 4
this is a reasonable assumption. Water sales and new connections are ex-
pected to increase, as more water becomes available for consumption through
the savings in unaccounted for water.
Water Production
13.       Average 3annual production from the existing facilities is estimated
at 6.64 million m /year. With the compleSion of the early action program this
is expected to increase to 6.91 million m /year in 1977 but with no further
significant increases thersafter until 1980 when the new3project will provide
increases to 7.0 million m /year going to 8.41 million m /year in 1985. Aver-
age water consumption and production statistics are summarized below:

C. CABANATUAN WATER DISTRICT
Service Area
14.       Cabanatuan City covers an area of 19,300 ha of which only 388 ha is
densely populated and lies within the present service area of the Cabanatuan
Water District (CWD). However, the consultants project population densities
in the present service area increasing by 1990 from the 1970 census figure of
140 persons/ha to 220/ha with an average population density of 98 persons/ha
in the larger 1990 service area. The Phase I project will provide improve-
ments to the present service area and extensions to meet 1985 needs. CWD
will also provide water supply facilities for low and middle income housing in
a proposed new suburb to Cabanatuan in the Barrio of Pangatian. By year 1990,
the CWD service area is expected to grow to over 1,250 ha with a population
of above 123,300. The served population is expected to increase from 25% in
1970 to about 70% in 1985 in these areas.



ANNEX 8
1-age r
Population
15.       Nueva Ecija Province had a 1970 population of 851,294 and an annual
growth rate over 1960-70 of 3.3%, somewhat higher than the national growth
rate of 3%. Cabanatuan City the former capital of the province had a growth
rate of 3.6% in this period and a 1970 population of 99,890 or 12% of that of
the Province. The poblacion had a 1970 population of 28,950 and the CWD
service area a population of 45,098 with a growth rate of 3.5%.
16.       In 1971, the Province had about 143,000 families, 21% classified as
urban. The city serves as an important trade center for the thriving rural
population. In 1974, there were 736 commercial wholesale and retail estab-
lishments and 75 medium and small scale industries. With the recent completion
of the nearby Pantabanguan Dam and reservoir designed for power, irrigation
and recreational use, the prospects for the local economy are favorable.
Local tourism has increased despite the poor sanitation, water and food
related diseases taking a heavy toll on health at a rate of 225 cases for
100,000 population and a death rate of 82 per 100,000, as reported in 1973.
There are no reliable statistics, however, on immigration, emigration, birth
and death rates on which to analyze the past population growth or to forecast
the future growth in CWD.
Population Projections
17.       In forecasting future population growth in CWD the consultants
took into account the expansion of the present service area, the Cabanatuan
City Framework Plan and the economic potential of the city. The past growth
rates in 24 barrios were studied and three projections made for the present
and additional 1990 and year 2000 service areas but for lack of data, only
one growth rate was assumed for each of these areas. In the densely populated
present service area the same growth rate as in the past decade of 3.5% is
assumed for this decade tapering down to 3.0% and 2.9% by 2000. A slightly
higher growth rate is assumed in the additional 1990 service area, at which
time the total projected population in the CWD service area will be 123,300,
as summarized below:

Population Served
18.       Except for a 1970 census statistic that 27% of the 16190 households
(an average occupancy of 6.17 persons/household) claimed to have been provided
with piped water from the city's water supply system, there are no reliable
records of population served by CWD. The consultants carried out a sampling
of 20 out of the 2251 connections served by CWD in September 1975, and ob-
tained an average of 9.5 persons served per connection. By comparison with
the census statistics it is then assumed, that for each connection there are
3.23 non-occupant users served by CWD. Because of the very limited 0.9%
sample this assumption should be reviewed before detailed design.
19.       In projecting future population served it is assumed that in the
years 1976-1978 the population served will be the same because of the limited
water availability. Though the number of connections will increase in these
years the number of non-occupant users per connection is expected to decrease
to nil, i.e., in 1979 it is assumed that all connections will serve only the
occupants of the households at 6.11 persons per household - near the 1970
average occupancy rate of 6.17 persons per household. As the new wells in
the project are connected to the system in 1979 and 1980 the number of users
per connection is expected to increase to 7.40 and 7.86 but then again
gradually decreased to about 6.86 in 1985 at which time 66% of the population
in the service area will be served by CWD.
Water Consumption
20.       There are no reliable data on domestic consumption.  The consultants
have used their findings in the neighboring city of Tarlac in estimating that



ANNEX 8
Page 7
domestic consumption is about 96 1/cd but will increase to 120 1/cd with im-
proved service and to 133 1/cd in 1985. These assumptions will be verified
before detailed design, in the light of the on-going metering program.
21.       As less than 40% of the commercial connections and only one of the
institutional connections are metered, and the meters are poorly maintained,
reliable data on institutional, commercial and industrial consumption are not
available. On the basis of a study of one month's meter readings in September
1975, the consultants estimate commercial, institutional and industrial consump-
tion to be about 58% of domestic consumption. Following a slight increase in
the early years, to meet suppressed demand, it is expected to reduce to 20%
in 1985.
Unaccounted for Water
22.       Unaccounted for water is estimated to be a high 33%.  Most of this
is from wastage and leakage at flat rate connections. With the completion of
the metering program in 1977, this is expected to be reduced substantially.
Leakage from the system is low at about 10% because of low pressures. Though
most of the older pipes will be replaced in this project and all services will
be metered it is not expected that unaccounted for water can be reduced to
below 25% by 1985.
Water Production
3

increases in production can be realized in 1977 and 1978 because of increased
pumping efficiency and good management. From then on average annual water
production keeps pace with demand as each of the new3wells in the project is
c4nnected to the system, increasing to 4.4 million m in 1982 and 5.4 million
m by 1985. Average water consumption and production statistics are summarized

D. LIPA WATER DISTRICT
Service Area
24.       Lipa Water District (LWD) covers the whole Lipa City which has a
total area of 20,600 ha. The present LWD service area is only 510 ha covering
the poblacion (130 ha) and ten adjacent barrios. By 1990 the LWD service area
is expected to increase to 1295 ha with a projected population of 25,400. The
Phase I project will provide improvements to existing areas areas and exten-
sions to new areas in meeting 1985 needs. The population density in the
poblacion is expected to grow from (1970 census) 137 persons/ha to 235
persons/ha with a density of 32 persons/ha in the newer areas. Served popu-
lation in these areas is expected to increase from an average of 18% in
1976 to 44% in 1985.
Population
25.       The 1970 population of Lipa City was 93,970, about 10% of that of
Batangas Province (pop: 928,300) in which it is situated. Over the past
decade (1960-70) the province had an annual population growth rate of 3.05%
very to close to the 3% national growth rate. Lipa City had a growth rate
of 3.4% per annum in this period. Lipa City poblacion had a growth rate of
3.6%, somewhat higher than that of the 3.4% growth rate in the LWD service
area.
26.       Natural increase accounted for 87% of the population growth in Lipa
City the rest being immigration. Reliable demographic information for LWD
service area is however not available and the consultants have based the
population projections on a few local economic indicators.
Population Projections
27.       Lipa City has excellent volcanic soils and an agricultural economy.
In 1975 there were about 1500 commercial establishments and some small cottage
industries. There are no major industries near the city or are any expected
to be established in the near future. In 1973, the 15 clinics and hospitals
in the city reported 518 cases and 27 deaths per 100,000 population of water
and food related diseases. Health data for the LWD area however is not
available.
28.       The past growth rates in 23 adjacent barrios and the poblacion
were studied and three projections made for the present, additional 1990 and
2000 service areas. Only one assumption of growth was made for each of these
geographical areas. Because of the increasing congestion, the growth rate in
the poblacion is expected to slowly decrease from 3.6% to 3.5% and 2.0% in
the next decade. Outside the poblacion a temporary decrease is expected in
this decade from 3.2% to 3.0% to be followed by an increase to 3.5% in the
next decade. Growth in the 1990 extension of the service area is also ex-
pected to slow down from 3.9% to 3.5% and 3.0% in the next decade. The
projections covering the period up to year 1990 are summarized below:

Population Served
29.       It is calculated from the 1970 census statistics that on an average
there were 7.55 occupants per household in Lipa City. The consultants made a
sampling of 20 out of the 714 connections existing in 1975 and obtained an
average of 12.75 users per connection and conclude that there are 5.2 non-
occupant users for each connection. It is assumed that over the years, with
improved availability of water, increased tariffs and the metering of all
service connections there would be a marked reduction in the number of non-
occupant users.
30.       In projecting future population served, it is assumed that though
the number of connections will increase in the years 1976-78 the population
served will be the same, because of limited water availability. As the new
wells in the project are connected to the system in about 1980, population
served would increase sharply from an average of 18% in the previous 5 years
to 25%, going on to 44% in 1985 as shown below:

Water Consumption
31.       The available data on domestic water consumption was obtained by
sampling 19 domestic connections over eight days before the impact of the full
metering program and increased tariffs. The consumption of 93 1/cd is there-
fore revised downwards to about 60 1/cd. Following the completion of the
project this is expected to double to 120 1/cd with small increases thereafter
to 126 1/cd in 1985. These assumptions will be verified from available data
nearing the completion of the metering program in early 1977.
32.       Data on non-domestic use such as commercial and institutional con-
sumption is non-existent. Based upon studies in other water districts the
commercial and institutional demand is assumed by the consultants to be 25%
of domestic consumption but gradually reducing to 15% by 1985. At the present
time LWD has no industrial consumers.
33.       As the system is not yet fully metered and a leakage survey will
not be done until late 1977 it is not possible to know now the amount or
to categorize unaccounted for water. The consultants, on the basis of the
available data on production and consumption, estimate this to be a very high
66% of production, about half of which is believed to be lost through leaks
in the distribution mains and service connections. By the time of the com-
pletion of the Phase I project unaccounted for water is expected to be halved
and thereafter reduced to 25% of production in year 1985.
Water Production
34.       Because of the recent failure of the pump in the market place well
(see Anne! 7 Part D), average annual water production for 1976 is a low
740,000 m . In 1977, this well is expected to be brought back into service,
and improvements made in the supply from the other wells. During 1978-80, the
new wells in the project will cause increments in production rising to 1.57
million m in 1982 and 2.2 million m by 1985. Average water production and
consumption statistics are summarized below:

E. LUCENA, PAGBILAO, TAYABAS WATER DISTRICT
Service Area
35.       The Lucena, Pagbilao, Tayabas Water District (LPTWD) was created out
of Lucena City, the capital of Quezon Province and the adjoining municipali-
ties of Pagbilao and Tayabas all three of which are supplied with water from
one principal source, the May-it spring. The present LPTWD service area of
513 ha covers the densely populated portions of these three urban areas and
some strip development on the transmission pipelines. By 1990 the LPTWD
service area is projected to increase to 945 ha with a population of 130,000-
and by year 2,000 to 2,400 ha with a total population of 222,600. The popula-
tion density in the three poblacions is expected to grow from (1970 census)
155 persons to 218 persons per ha in this period. In areas adjacent to the
poblacions of the three urban areas population density is expected to increase
from 24 persons to 55 persons/ha. Served population by LPTWD is expected to
increase from the current 51,600 to 72,100 in 1985, the period in the Phase
I project. The project will provide improvements to existing areas and
extensions to new areas in meeting 1985 needs.
Population
36.       Though LPTWD is formed out of three municipalities there appears
to be relationship between population growths of Quezon Province (1970 pop:
983,300) and Lucena City and the two Municipalities of Pagbilao and Tayabas
(total 1970 pop: 138,760). Over the past decade (1960-70) the province had
a high annual population growth rate of 4.1%. The growth rate in the future
LPTWD service area for the same period was also 4.1%. The growth rate of the
province is higher than the 3% national growth rate and is unusual in com-
parison with the other provinces discussed in the consultants' reports.
Again, unlike LPTWD the growth rates in urban areas of each province are
usually higher than that of the provinces. No analysis is available at this
time to explain these unusual features in LPTWD but it was observed that the
local economy which is dependent on coconut and fishing, with some minor
industries and tourism appears to be thriving.
37.       Persons born in the three LPTWD areas represented 77% of the total
1970 population. Those born in other parts of Quezon Province were about 10%
and the remainder were migrants from elsewhere. As reliable demographic data
are not available, the consultants did not find it feasible to make projec-
tions on high medium and low assumptions of growth for LPTWD. Only one
projection, based upon available local economic indicators was made.
Population Projections
38.       Agriculture is the principal industry, with coconut plantations
occupying nearly half of the total land areas of Lucena, Pagbilao, and Tayabas.
Tayabas supplies about 75% of the lime used by the country. Lucena has a
coconut-chemical plasticizer factory and a pioneering coconut oil industry.
Pagbilao and Lucena have growing fishing industries and Lucena some local
tourism.



ANNEX 8
Page 12
39.       In 1973, the six hospitals and clinics in Lucena City reported
548 cases and 143 deaths per 100,000 population of water and food related
diseases. Most of these were from "gastroenteritis". Health data for the
LPTWD area is however unavailable.
40.       The population projections for Lucena, Pagbilao and Tayabas make
the following assumptions:
(i) Lucena
The population growth in the present service area would be
2.5% per annum (a little below the 2.6% of the past decade)
until 1980 and reduce to 2.0% thereafter. In the future
service area it is assumed that the 6.1% annual growth rate
of the past decade will not be sustained and would be 4.0%
in the period 1970-80 and reduce to 3.5% thereafter.
(ii) Pagbilao
The annual population growth rate of 1.7% of the past decade
would reduce to 1.0% over 1970-90 in the present service area.
In the future service area this is assumed to be 6.0% per
annum, a little lower than the 6.2% of the past decade.
(iii) Tayabas
The growth rate of 3.7% per annum of the past decade is
assumed to drop sharply to 1.0% per annum during 1970-90.
In the future service area the growth rate of 7.7% per annum
of the past decade is assumed to drop a little to 7.0% per
annum up to 1980 and drop again to 6.0% per annum thereafter.
41.       The population in the 1990 LPTWD service area is projected as
follows:

Population Served
42.       The consultants estimate that on an average about 9.7 persons are
now served by each connection. The census figures and studies by the con-
-sultants however indicate that in LPTWD there are only about 6.4 to 6.1
persons resident in each household, the difference arising from borrowers
of water from adjoining households without service connections. In the
projections it is assumed that the population served by each connection will
decrease from 9.4 to 6.2 over 1976-85. In the early years of the project not
many connections can be given because of limited water availabiltiy and the
percentage of population served is expected to decrease. After the completion
of the project however the served population will rapidly increase from 51% to
62% as shown below:

Water Consumption
43.       Because of non-availability of water consumption data the consul-
tants have based domestic consumption on experience in other water districts
similar to LPTWD. It is assumed that the present domestic consumption is
125 1/cd in Lucena City and 112 1/cd in Tayabas and Pagbilao giving an aver-
age of 118 1/cd for the WD. Though these assumptions are relatively higher
than these for the other WDs in the project they are based on the somewhat
higher level of economic activity in the area and will be verified from new
data nearing the completion of the metering program in early 1977. It is
projected that, except for a big jump of 7 1/cd between 1979-81 when the
project nears completion, that domestic consumption will increase uniformly
to 136 1/cd by 1985.
44.       Because Lucena City is the provincial capital there is some commer-
cial and institutional consumption but because of the non-availability of
data this is assumed to be 15% of domestic consumption. There are a few small
industries in the area and industrial demand is assumed to increase uniformly
from 4% in 1975 to 6.6% in 1985. Both these assumptions are based on judg-
ment and will require study before detailed design.



ANNEX 8
Page 14
45.       The consultants estimated unacounted for water to be 83% of produc-
tion in 1975. Since then the LPTWD has started metering and repaired some
of the leaks and unaccounted for water is now estimated to be 64% of produc-
tion, of which about 26% is leakage. With the improvements in the project
and under the present management, unaccounted for water is expected to be
reduced to 30% in 1985.
Water Production
46.       The present annuil average water productign in LPTWD is estimated
to be about 7.35 million m of which 1.23 million m is water of poor quality.
It is however proposed to phase out this unsatisfactory source and to replace
it with good quality water under the project as can be seen in the water
production and consumption statistics summarized below:
