F.  SAN FERNANDO (LA UNION) WATER DISTRICT
Service Area
47.       The San Fernando (La Union) Water District (LUWD) is situated on
the west-central coast of La Union Province (1970 pop: 373,680) in north-
western Luzon. The present LUWD service area of 215 ha includes only the
more densely populated sections of San Fernando, the provincial capital and
the adjoining municipality of San Juan. The future service area will include
the urban sections in the adjacent municipalities of Bacnotan, San Gabriel
and Bauang which have no piped water supply service. By 1990, the LUWD
expects to provide service to some 2,140 ha in these five municipalities.
In San Fernando, the population density in the poblacion is expected to
increase from 98 persons/ha to about 178 persons/ha. A slightly lower
growth rate is expected in the San Juan poblacion. Served population is
expected to increase from the current 12% to 36% in 1985 - the period in
the Phase I project.



ANNEX 8
Page 15
Population
48.       Agriculture, particularly rice and tobacco cultivation with some
coconut near the seashore, is the principal industry in the province. Pisci-
culture is of local importance in San Fernando, San Juan and Bacnotan. There
are two cement factories, and a tobacco redrying plant at Bacnotan and a soft
drink bottling plant in San Fernando. Small scale agro-industries like cigar
making and weaving are widespread as are other minor cottage industries. The
scenic coastline has caused an increase in local tourism and a proliferation
of beach resorts between Bauang and San Fernando.
49.       There is no information on the incidence of water and food related
diseases in LUWD. However, in 1973, for the whole province there were re-
ported 557 cases and 35 deaths per 100,000 population from such causes.
50.       In the absence of reliable demographic data, the population projec-
tions for the five municipalities prepared by the consultants generally take
into account the above factors in the assumption of future population growth.
The projections for the expected 1990 service areas for each of the five
municipalities make the following assumptions:
(i) San Fernando and San Juan
The annual rate of population growth of La Union Province over
the past decade was 2.4% and well below the national growth
rate of 3%. In 1970 the population of all five municipali-
ties was 133,200, 36% of that of the province, and population
in the presnt LUWD service area only 20,500 with an annual
growth rate of 3.5% in San Fernando and 2.2% in San Juan pob-
lacions. In the future, San Fernando poblacion is expected
to grow at about 3.0% and San Juan at 2.5% increasing to 3.0%
per annum after 1980. In the suburbs of these poblacions, to
be provided with future water service, the annual rate of
growth is expected to be 4.0% dropping to 3.5% after 1980 in
San Fernando and 2.5% increasing to 3.0% after 1980 in San Juan.
(ii) Bauang
The annual rate of population growth is expected to be 2.0% in
the poblacion but 3.5% in the suburbs up to 1980 increasing to
2.5% and 4.0% respectively after the provision of piped water
supply.
(iii)  Bacnotan
In Bacnotan the annual rate of population growth in the pob-
lacion is projected to be 3.5% up to 1990. The suburbs are
however expected to have an increase in the growth rate from
3.0% to 3.5% in 1980 after the provision of piped water supply.



ANNEX 8
Page 16
(iv) San Gabriel
This is a very small village on the water supply transmission
pipeline (but not now supplied by LUWD) which is expected
to grow at 2.0% per annum up to 1980 after which a somewhat
higher growth rate is expected, following the provision of
piped water supply.
52.       The population in the LUWD 1990 service area is projected as follows:

Water Consumption
53.       Because of non-availability of water consumption data the consultants
have based water consumption projections on information obtained from inter-
views with 30 consumers, the 1970 census and the August 1975 records of
metered consumption. From the interviews it is deduced that the average
number of consumers for connection in San Fernando and San Juan are 8.95
and 6.0 respectively. The 1970 census indicates that the average number
of persons per household in San Juan was 6.04 in that year. In August 1975
there were only 378 metered connections compared with almost 700 metered
connections in September 1976. These later statistics are therefore used
in the present projections.
54.       Domestic consumption is assumed to be 118 1/cd, the same as in the
Lucena, Pagbilao, Tayabas Water District which is similar to LUWD in many
ways. Except for a slightly higher increase in 1980 when the project
benefits come on stream this consumption is expected to increase gradually
to 138 1/cd in 1985. It is estimated that commercial and industrial con-
sumption, all of which is not now fully recorded, is about 80% of domestic
consumption and will reduce to about 34% in 1985. The number of persons
served, taken as an average of 8.2 persons per connection for 1976 is only
expected to reduce to 7.9 persons per connection in 1985 because of the



ANNEX 8
Page 17
newer areas to be brought into the system. Unaccounted for water is now
estimated to be 60% of production and with improvements following a leakage
survey is expected to reduce to 25% by 1985.
Water Production
55.       Annual average water production from the present spring sources is
governed by the hydraulic capacity of the transmission pipelines and therefore
cannot increase from the estimated 1.44 million m until 1980 when the first
well in the proposed new source development will produce water. The water
production and consumption statistics for the project period are summarized
below:

G. TARLAC WATER DISTRICT
Service Area
56.       Tarlac Water District (TWD) covers the municipality of Tarlac,
(1970 pop: 135,130) the capital of Tarlac province in central Luzon. The
present TWD service area covers only 273 ha made up of the poblacion and six
contiguous barrios having a population density of 133 persons/ha in 1970.
The consultants project an increase to 189 persons/ha in the present service
area and 39 persons/ha in the projected extensions to the service area by
1990. In this period the service area is expected to grow to over 2000 ha.
The Phase I project provides improvements to the service in the present
service area and extensions to meet 1985 needs. The served population is
expected to increase from 6% in 1976 to 56% in 1985 in the future service
area.
Population
57.       Tarlac Province had a 1970 population of 559,700 and an annual
growth rate over 1960-70 of 2.7%, somewhat lower than the national growth
rate of 3% for the same period. The rate of population growth in the
present service area for this period was an unusually low 2.5% in comparison
with some of the other cities in the project.



ANNEX 8
Page 18
58.       In 1971, the Province had 93,800 families, 16% classified as urban.
Agriculture is the main industry, the two major crops being rice and sugar cane.
Tarlac Municipality is the commercial and government center for the province.
Except for some small scale activities like shoe making, pottery and handi-
crafts there are no major industries in Tarlac. Water and food related
diseases for the whole province had a heavy toll on health, at a rate of 146
cases per 100,000 and a death rate of 23 per 100,000, as reported in 1973.
There are no reliable statistics on immigration, emigration, birth and death
rates to analyze past population growth or accurately forecast future growth
in TWD.
Population Projections
59.       Future population growth in TWD is based upon past growth rates
which are projected into the future with small adjustments for increased
density or additional expansion. In the present service area the rate of
growth is assumed to be 2.0%, one half percent lower than the 2.5% rate of
the past decade. This is expected to further decrease to 1.5% after 1980.
In the extended service areas however a very slightly higher rate of 3.0%
(over the 2.9% of the past decade) is assumed by the consultants. The pop-
ulation projection for the period 1970-85 is shown below:

Water Demand Projections
61.       Estimation of domestic consumption is based upon one month's meter
readings in 1975 of 82 domestic connections which yielded a unit per capita
consumption of 96 l/cd. In 1976 this was corrected to 100 1/cd as more meters
were installed. On the assumption that with improved pressures that consump-
tion will increase, the consultants project per capita consumption in 1985 to
be 134 1/cd.
62.       Commercial and institutional consumption is not fully metered and
is therefore not known. On the basis of an assumption that unaccounted for
water is about 50% it is estimated that commercial and institutional consump-
tion is currently 77% of domestic consumption and is projected to reduce to
20% by 1985. There are no industrial connections in TWD.
63.       Unaccounted for water is made of leakage, and public use such as
fire fighting, mains flushing and street cleaning. By ascribing values to
each of these causes for unaccounted for water this is estimated to be about
50%. By 1985 TWD expects to reduce unaccounted for water to 25% of production.
64.       In 1975 the consultants estimated water production to be 1,575 m 3
day. Since then TWD has made, with LWUA help, improvements to the pumping
arrangements and productisn is expected to be able to meet at a minimum aver-
age day iemand of 2,250 m /day in 1978. In 1979 the first of the new 44 1/sec
(3,800 m /day) wells is expected3to be brought into service to meet the pro-
jected peak hour demand of 690 m /day (69 1/sec). The project will increase
total produtcion of 16,000 m /day (184 I/sec) adequate to meet the maximum
day demand in 1985 (See Annex 7, para 110). Average water production and
consumption statistics are summarized below:

PHILIPPINES
PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
The Project Water Districts
Baguio
1.        Baguio is a chartered city and is situated on the western slopes of
the Central Cordillera mountain range in Benguet Province on the island of
Luzon.   Ground surface elevations within the city range from about 900 meters
to over 1,600 meters. The average monthly precipitation for the wet season
(May - October) is 518 mm and for the dry season 56 mm. Average temperatures
(during 1951-1970) vary from a high of about 20 C in May to a low of about 18 C
in January. Baguio's economy depends principally on tourism. It is a resort
city for local vacationers and foreign tourists. It is also an educational
center.
2.        Baguio WD was formed in June 1975 and received a Conditional Certi-
ficate of Conformance from LWUA in August 1975. The city's present population
is around 100,000 of which about 56% have access to piped water.  Almost all
of the 7,000 connections are metered. The Water District has an active Board,
headed by a successful businessman, who is keenly interested in improving
water supply to this popular tourist city. The General Manager's position is
currently vacant and the Board is looking for a suitable candidate. Under
its general conditions of lending LWUA requires that the WDs submit to it the
names and biographical data of its General Manger and Departmental Heads and
give serious consideration to LWUA's advice on all matters relating to the
technical and managerial qualifications of its personnel. LWUA will thus be
consulted on the selection of a new General Manager.
3.        Baguio WD's organization follows the general pattern as shown in
Annex 11. Currently, it employs about 120 persons in its administrative,
commercial, production, construction and maintenance and engineering divisions.
The financial and accounting aspects are the responsibility of the Finance
Officer and the Accountant under the Chief of the Administrative Division.
The present staff, taken over on the district's formation is somewhat large
but will be fully utilized as the district's activities increase.
Cabanatuan
4.        Cabanatuan is situated in the central part of Nueva Ecija, the
eastern most province in Central Luzon. The city is a broad plain with an
average altitude of 29 m above sea level.  Its annual average rainfall based
on the 20 year period of 1951-70 is about 1,780 mm and mean temperature 27.6C.
Its 1976 population is about 78,000. Agriculture is the most important sector
of Cabanatuan's economy. The commercial and industrial activities consist
mainly of wholesale and retail businesses and manufacture of furniture and
farm implements. Cabanatuan has also become a stopover for travellers to
North Luzon and provides tourist facilities.



ANNEX 12
Page 2
5.        Cabanatuan WD was formed in November 1974 and was issued a CCC by
LWUA in April 1975. The WD serves about 21,000 people through some 2,500
connections of which about 60% are expected to be metered by the end of 1976.
Its General Manager, with an agricultural degree and business background is a
dynamic person and has recruited some good young staff for the commercial and
accounting divisions. The Board is generally guided by and relies on the
General Manager. This is one of the better-run districts; has 4 main divisions
- Administrative, Commercial, Construction and Maintenance and Engineering;
employs 44 persons and is organized on the standard pattern shown in Annex 11.
Lipa
6.        Lipa is one of the two main cities in Batangas, a highland volcanic
province in the southwestern region of Luzon Island. It is located in a ter-
race with an elevation of about 310 m above sea level. The city is flanked
by a high mountain mass to the east and Taal Lake (at near sea level) to
the West. Its average annual rainfall during the period 1971-74 was about
2,5000mm and monthly temperatures ranged from a minimum of 200C to a maximum
of 31 C. Lipa City's present population is about 54,000 and agriculture is
its principal industry.
7.        The Lipa City Water District was formed in November 1974 and was
granted a CCC by LWUA in January 1975. In September 1976, it had about 760
service connections of which about 300 were metered and served about 17% of
the population.
8.        The WD's General Manager is a qualified engineer; the district
presently employs 28 persons and its organization pattern is the same as in
other districts, except that the production, operations, maintenance and
engineering are handled by a single division. Lipa City WD also maintains
small handpumps in the neighboring villages for which it employs 4 technicians
whose costs are met by the city administration.
Lucena-Pagbilao-Tayabas
9.        Lucena is the capital of the Province of Quezon and the WD also
covers the adjacent municipalities of Tayabas and Pagbilao. Lucena and Tayabas
are located in the coastal plains at elevations from 5 to 30 meters and Tayabas
lies in the foothills of Mount Banahaw at an elevation of about 200 meters.
The average annual rainfall is about 2,000 mm and mean temperature 270C.
Agriculture is the principal industry and coconut plantations occupy nearly
half the land area. Tayabas supplies about 75% of the country's lime. Lucena
has a pioneering coco oil factory.
10.       The WD was formed in January 1975 and was granted LWUA's CCC in
flay 1975. It serves some 51,600 people (about 56% of the served area popula-
tion) through about 5,500 connections of which only about 500 were metered in
August 1976. The present General Manager is old and is due for retirement in
February 1977. The Chairman of the Board (who is the Chief Engineer of the



ANNEX 12
Page i)
local Public Works Department) has to assume a major role in the District s
operation. A suitable replacement for the General Manager's position will
probably have to be recruited from outside the present staff of the WD. The
Water District's organization follows the general pattern and it employs 57
persons. The Finance Officer and the Accountant, recruited recently, have
yet to come to grips with the Distrist's problems. Particular attention will
have to be devoted by LWUA to upgrading the efficiency of this WD to an
acceptable level.
San Fernando (La Union)
11.       San Fernando is situated along the  China Sea on the We-st-Central
coast of La Union, a narrow province along the Northwestern coast of Luzon
Island. The Water District includes the municipalities of San Fernando3, the
provincial capital; San Juan and Bacnotan on the North; San Gabriel on the
Northeast and Bauang on the South. San Gabriel has a h Lly terrain with
elevations from 30 to 400 meters but the other municipalities can generaliy
be classified as coastal lowlands ranging from 10 to 140 meters.  The Le
perature in the area ranges between 25 C and 27 0C. The economy is mainly
agricultural (tobacco and coconut) but some industrial plants - 2 cement
factories, a beverage plant and a tobacco redrying plant - have been con-
structed in recent years; fishponds have also been developed in the area.
San Fernando is also a tourist center.
12.       The Water District was formed in September 1974 and obtained CCC
from LWUA In April 1975. At present it serves about 11% of the estimated
service area population of about 65,000 through some 900 connections of which
about 80% are metered.  Its present staff numbers 24 and its organization is
similar to that in Lipa. The Chairman of the Board, a local businessman,
takes keen interest in the District and devotes adequate time to it. The
General Manager is an engineer who lacks financial background. San F'ernando
WD will need special attention, particularly for upgrading the efficiency of
its administrative, financial and accounting divisions.
Tarlac
13.       Tarlac municipality is situated in the east-central part ot Tarl.i
a landlocked province in central Luzon and is the provincial capital. The
city is generally flat, with a few hills to the west and northeast and has
an elevation of about 40 meters. Its average monthly rainfall ranges from
zero during the dry period to about 600 mm during the rainy period. Its
economy is mainly agricultural and the two major crops are rice and sugar.
Industrial activity is limited to small-scale industries such as shoe-making,
pottery and handicrafts.



ANNEX 12
Page 4
14.       Tarlac WD was formed in July 1974 and received LWUA's CCC in
February 1975. The served area's population is about 89,000 of which appro-
ximately 6% are presently served by the WD through some 800 connections of
which about 150 were metered in August 1976. It has 3 divisions - Adminis-
trative, Commercial and Operations and Maintenance. The total staff numbers
about 20. The Chairman of the Board, an attorney by profession, takes per-
sonal interest in the WD and has provided rent free accommodation to it in
his office building. The General Manager is a capable engineer and a good
manager.



ANNEX 13
Page 1
PHILIPPINES
PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Justification for Metering
1.        The decision on how much (if any) metering should be introduced
into a particular water supply system should be subjected to a cost-benefit
analysis. In areas where water is inexpensive to produce and where easily
accessible ground or surface sources are abundant relative to the expected
population growth, the cost of universal metering may well exceed the benefits
from the induced conservation. These costs include the initial meter purchase
and installation and the recurrent maintenance, reading and billing expenses.
Benefits accrue from the conservation effects of metering which can free more
water for use by other consumers (e.g., those with lower pressures) and/or
permit savings in capital and operating costs by reducing the overall demands
on the system. Metering may also facilitate leak detecton, although its in-
cremental contribution to that end is probably small if the basic distribution
system is in poor repair. However, these benefits arising from decreased
water consumption by the metered individuals must be reduced by the value
to them of the lost water. As long as it is not pure wastage, the consumer
will be suffering some comparative loss when he is asked to pay a price which
is more than the water's marginal value to him.
Metering Costs
2.        The typical 1/ half-inch meter used in the project districts costs
P 126 and is expected to last about five years (with only breakdown mainte-
nance) and is then replaced. The installation or meter mounting cost is about
P 25, and periodic reading and billing costs were estimated in Baguio and
Cabanatuan 2/ at P 2.50 and P 2.70 per meter per month, respectively. Assum-
ing these figures to be representative, the annual equivalent cost per meter
(using a 12% discount rate) can be calculated as follows:
1/   One-half inch meters accounted for an average 94% of connections
in the six cities.
2/   The other cities either were just beginning their metering program
or lacked reliable historical information.

Conservation Benefits
3.        To calculate how much consumption would have to be reduced to offset
this expense one needs the marginal supply cost of producing water in each of
the six cities. As detailed in Annex 14 and summarized in Table 1, Column (1)
in para 4 below, this average incremental cost (AIC) varies considerably, from

before (i.e., of zero value to the consumer) then the corresponding reduc-
tions in consumption per month which would be necessary to justify metering

Cost-Benefit Comparison
4.        To determine the likelihood of such a reduction in consumption,
a comparison must be made between metered and unmetered consumers. However,
reliable consumption information on unmetered consumers is naturally difficult
to find. In Lipa City WD the first month's consumption of 46 newly metered
connections was available, and this should provide a good indication (perhaps
somewhat conservative) of what consumption was prior to metering since the
consumer had not yet received a bill under the new system which would induce
him to change his consumption pattern.

The average consumption per connection was 63 m , although the mean was 42 m
due to very high consumption by several households which were subsequently
discovered to have badly leaking pipes and/or fixtures. By way of comparison,
the city with the most metered connsctions in existence, Baguio, recorded an
average monthly conslmption of 21 m , while Cabanatuan's average of 360 slch
connections3was 33 m . San Fernando (La Union) showed an average of 30 m and
Tarlac 23 m on their respective metered connections. These various figures
are not strictly comparable, of course, since income levels, water charges and
many other factors vary across cities. For example, Baguio has the highest
average income but also a water charge which is currently more than double
that of any of the other cities (due mostly to more difficult supply condi-
tions). Nevertheless, a rough compa ison can be made between average monthly3
household consumptions of 20 to 35 m  for metered connections and around 60 m
for non-metered consumption.
5.     ,4 Thus, it appears highly probable that the necessary minimal reduction
of 1-4 mj/month could be expected in response to metering. The cost of this
reduction to the consumer can be estimated by noting that such a reduction
would amount to only around 5% of the original consumption. Thus, it is also
likely that the total value of such a relatively small amount is near zero.
In other words, even if the consumer's demand curve were so steep that the
average value of that lost water was half its AIC the corresponding reduction
necessary to justify metering (i.e., twice the figures shown in Table 1,
Column (2) of para 4) could still be expected to take place. The general
conclusion therefore is that metering is clearly justified for the 6 districts
included in this project.

Internal Economic Rates of Return and Average Incremental Costs
1.        The incremental return on investment or internal economic rate of
return (IER) is the discount rate which equates the present values of the
cost and benefit streams attributable to the investment. Project justifica-
tion is then determined by comparing this rate with the opportunity cost of
capital in the economy. Whten the benefits of a water supply project are
minimally approximated by the revenue generated, the same input information
can be used to calculate the average incremental cost (AIC) of the water soiK.
The AIC is the cost per cubic mieter of the incremental water sold, averaged
over the project's life and discounted back to the present using the oppor-
tunity cost of capital. Thus if the price of water is set equal to its AIC,
the internal economic rate of return on the project will just equal the
opportunity cost of capital.
2.        Tie assumptions used i-o calculate the IER and AIC for the six
project cities are listed below.
(1)  All costs are in constant (i977) prices, exclusive of
taxes and duties.
(2)  Incremental :onsumptior and operating costs are taken
from Ainnexes ! -.'0 with all costs adjusted for inflation.
(3)  The shadow price of foreign exchange (in terms of pesos
per dollar) is 11% above its official rate.
(4)  The opportunity CuSt of capital is 12-15%.
(5)  The projects have a useful life of 40 years.
3.        Table" i Lil.        sh: O if'ow the input data for the six WDs based on
these assumptions, antd Table / sum-m arizes the results of the IER and AIC
calculations.

Assumptions for Financial Projections
General
The financial statements - Income Statements, Flow oW Funds State-
ments and Balance Sheets for the six Water Districts in Annexes 15ta), (b),
and (c) through 20 (a), (b) and (c), show the projected financial results
for the WDs' operations for the years 1976 through 1985. The basic assumptions
for these projections are given below.
Income Statements
Revenue
1.        Production and sales of water are according to the projections in
Annex 8.  Unaccounted for water which is around 50-66% in all the six WDs is
projected to decline to about 25% (30% in case of Lucena) by 1985, assuming
metering of all consumers and an efficient leakage and waste detection pro-
grams. Rate increases are assumed in 1977, 1979, 1980 and 1982, to generate
adequate funds (a) during the project construction period (1977-1981), to
make a constribution of about 10% to the proposed investment, and (b) after
project completion to meet the debt service and leave a reasonable surplus
for normal extension and improvement of the system.
The average rates adopted in the financial projections are given
below. The increases in 1977 are partly due to increases in tariffs and
partly to expected improvement in the efficiency of meter readings and
metering of unmetered connections:
Lucena-       San
Lipa    Pagbilao-    Fernando
Baguio  Cabanatuan   City    Tayabas        (La Union)    Tarlac
1976         1.42      0.49        0.44       0.30        0.66          0.62
1977         1.70      0.80        0.70       0.50        1.00          1.20
1978         1.70      0.80        0.70       0.50        1.00          1.20
1979          1.70     1.00        1.40       0.80         1.80         1.60
1980         2.00      1.50        1.60       1.20        1.80          1.60
1981         2.00      1.50        1.60       1.20        1.80          1.60
1982         2.80      2.30        2.60       2.30        3.30          2.20
1983         2.80      2.30        2.60       2.30        3.30          2.20
1984         2.80      2.30        2.60       2.30        3.30          2.20
1985         2.80      2.30        2.60       2.30        3.30          2.20



ANNEX 22
Page 2
2.        Other operational revenue comprises mainly the miscellaneous rev-
enue items e.g., connection and reconnection fees, penalties for delayed
payments, supervision fees etc., and are projected to increase by about 5% -
10% annually.
Operating Expenses
3.        Staff costs (wages) are projected to increase 10% annually during
1977 through 1981 (25% for Cabanatuan in 1977 to meet expected increases in
staff remuneration) to cover escalation. The WDs do not propose to employ
additional staff until after completion of the project, and the current staff
is orisidered adequate to meet the operating requirements till about 1981.
A 201% increase in wages is projected annually in 1982 through 1985 - 10%
towards employment of additional staff and 10% for escalation of prices.
4.        Chemicals are projected at 8 pesos per 1,000 m3 of water produc-
tion in 1976 and are escalated 10% annually.
5.        Power costs are projected to increase with the increase in water
production and are escalated 10% annually. In Baguio, power costs per m
of water produced are estimated to increase between 4 and 7% in 1978-1981
(inclusive of escalation of 10%) due to expected cheaper cost of hydro-
electric power and greater use of water supplied by gravity from Santo
Thomas Reservoir. In San Fernando (La Union) WD, power costs during
lP982-1985 are based on the estimated power consumption for the new source
of supply.
6.        Materials for maintenance are projected to increase about 10%
annually through 1979 (through 1980 for Baguio and San Fernando) and there-
after these costs are assumed at about 0.5% of the gross value of assets in
operation (about 1.2 to 2% in Baguio).
7.        Other expenses follow the projections under wages.
8.        Other indirect costs are the provisions for bad debts and are
assumed at 5% of water revenues.
9.        Depreciation has been calculated at 2.5% of the average gross
fixed assets in operation.
Cash Flow and Balance Sheet
10.       Contributions relate to customer contributions towards cost of
providing new service connections. The average estimated cost of a new
connection is 300 Pesos. It is assumed that 50% of the new consumers will
pay the cost of connections in lump and the remaining 50% will elect to pay
in installments over a five-year period with an appropriate interest charge
(interest charge ignored for projections).



ANNEX zz
Page 3
11.       IBRD loan.  This represents the Bank loan which will be re-lent
by LWUA to the respective WDs at LWUA's rate of 3/4 of 1% per month. Under
LWUA's terms interest unpaid at the end of the month is added to the principal
and earns interest at the same rate.
12.       National Loan.  This is the projected LWUA's loan to WDs to cover
local costs of the project to the extent not met from internal cash generation
(estimated at about 10% of the investment including IDC) and includes interest
during construction.
13.       Other Loans.  These are the small loans from LWUA to WDs made in
1975 and 1976 mainly for water meters (and other minor works in Baguio and
San Fernando WDs) and are assumed repayable in five equal annual installments
at LWUA'S interest rate.
14.       Investment in project follows the project costs in Annex 4.
15.       Capitalized interest represents the interest during construction
of the project.
16.       Other works are the non-project works in 1976 and in 1982 through
1985.
17.       Amortization.  For the Bank loan re-lent by LWUA to WDs and for
LWUA's loans to WDs for local expenditures on the project, the debt amorti-
zation is assumed in equal annual payments in 30 years inclusive of a grace
period of 4-1/2 years, at the LWUA's present interest rate of 3/4 of 1% per
month.
18.       Accounts receivable are assumed at about 1-1/2% months billing.
19.       Inventories are projected to be about three months consumption of
materials and chemicals.

PHIL TPPINES
PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
LWUA's Guidelines for Water Rates
1.        On the recommendation of its consultants, James M. Montgomery,
LWUA has formulated the following guidelines for observance by the local
Water Districts in fixing and reviewing water rates. The basic objective
is that the WDs should operate on a self supporting basis, without any
subsidy from Government.
2.        The Water District is required to compute its projected revenue
requirements for the next year, and determine (on the basis of historical
and other information) what percentage of it will be derived from unmetered
and metered consumers. Since most connections in the six project cities are
expected to be metered during 1977 the following describes the metered tariff
only.
3.        Water consumption through all 3metered connections is converted into
revenue units (RU). For the first 10 m of consumption, the following factors
are adopted for this conversion:
Size of Connection                  Ratio to 3/8" Connection
Domestic and        Commercial and
Government           Industrial          Wholesale
3/8"/1                1.0                   2.0                 3.0
1/2"                  2.5                   5.0                 7.5
3/4"                  4.0                   8.0                12.0
1"                  8.0                  16.0                24.0
1-1/2"                 20.0                  40.0                60.0
2"                 50.0                 100.0               150.0
3"                 90.0                 180.0               270.0
4"                180.0                 360.0               540.0
/1 A 3/8" connection is actually a 1/2" connection regulated by an
orifice to give the discharge of a 3/8" connection. The minimum
size of unmetered connections is therefore 1/2" and equal to 2.5 RUs.
3 3
Each m  of water consumption in excess of 10  is weignted as I RU for do-
mestic and government, 2 RUs for commercial and industrial and 3 RUs for
wholesale connections but no distinction is made for consumption through
different pipe sizes. The RU total thus arrived at is then divided into the
total revenue requirement for arriving at the RU chare which forms the basis
for determining the service charge for the first 10 m (using the above
weights) and the commodity charge for subsequent consumption.



ANNEX 24
Page 2
3.        In practice, this method of rate determination has several drawbacks.
First, the present tariff structure results in higher average water costs for
the smaller consumers since about 95% of domestic connections are 1/2 inch,
and for them the first 10 m3 consumption is equal to 2.5 RU per m3 while
subsequent consumption is equal to one RU/m3. LWUA has proposed introducing a
3/8" connection (which is actually an orifice on a 1/2" outlet) for which the
consumer would be charged the same no district at present has been provided
with any 3/8" meters or constrictors 1/.
4.        Second, the practice of charging business consumers twice the re-
sidential rate for purposes of cross-subsidization can be counter-productive
unless the distinction between business and residential customers is very
carefully made. Problems with this distinction have already led to complaints
in one district from resident-owners of rooming houses who are paying double
for water which is used for essentially domestic purposes by their student-
boarders (to whom the rate is actually passed on). There are also many small
"commercial establishments" which are really storefront houses whose occupants
have no higher income than the non-self-employed and whose water is used
almost entirely for domestic purposes. Many of the small businesses in these
communities are operating in the highly-competitive informal sector where there
is little or no scope for passing on water rate increases to the customer.
Even where the increase is passed on the final burden may fall directly on
the lowest income classes depending upon the type of business. A more equit-
able method of cross-subsidization would be from the larger water consumers
(business or residential) to the smaller through a system of progressive rates
where the unit price of water increases along with the quantity consumed.
5.        Third, the level of the tariff should be based on incremental
future costs, not average historical costs. Unless the real cost of supplying
additional water is transmitted to the consumer (by means of a tariff based
on incremental future costs) he will continue to increase his consumption as
though future water sources were as inexpensive to develop as the past one.
This is clearly not true for these six districts; in every case the marginal
(future) cost per m3 is substantially higher than the average cost per m3.
6.        Finally, the cost of the initial connection to the public system
averages around P 300 plus individual house fittings of about P 100-200. For
the poorer consumers this may represent a very large outlay so that an install-
ment plan to spread the cost over five years has been discussed with LWUA.
All of the districts and LWUA have agreed in principal to this proposal, and
it is estimated to affect 50% of the new consumers.
1/   In addition, there is little economic justification for charging less
for 3/8" connections since there is no cost savings from smaller dis-
tribution lines with both the existing and proposed distribution
networks sized to accommodate system peaks and fire-fighting demands.



ANNEX 25

PROVINCIAL CITIES WATER SUPPLY PROJECT
Schedule of Water Rates 1/
Old         New         Date of Effect
Rates       Rates         of New Rates
-----Pesos/m7----
Domestic Metered Connections
Baguio                                    1.25          1.25             -
Cabanatuan                                0.40          0.50       Jan.   1, 1977
Lipa                                      0.30          0.50        Jan.   1, 1977
Lucena-Pagbilao-Tayabas                   0.25          0.40       April  1, 1977
San Fernando (La Union)                   0.28          0.50       Jan.   1, 1977
Tarlac                                    0.40          0.75       Nov.   1, 1976
Commercial and Industrial
Metered Connections
Baguio                                    1.50          2.50       Oct.   1, 1976
Cabanatuan                                0.80          1.00       Jan.   1, 1977
Lipa                                      0.60          1.00        Jan.   1, 1977
Lucena-Pagbilao-Tayabas                   0.40          0.80       April  1, 1977
San Fernando (La Union                    0.56          1.00       Jan.   1, 1977
Tarlac                                    0.80          1.50       Nov.   1, 1976
1/    The rates in this schedule are for the metered connections for consumption
exceeding 10 m3 per month. For first 10 m3 of monthly consumption a service
charge ranging from P 10.00 to P 18.75 is made for 1/2" domestic connections
in each WD. Domestic consumers with larger size connections pay higher
service charges and the service charge for commercial and industrial
consumers is twice the domestic rate (three times the domestic rate in
Lucena-Pagbilao-Tayabas WD). For unmetered connections, different rates are
applicable in each WD, increasing with the increase in the size of the
connections.  These connections will be metered in the coming year.
